This is a subdivision of his statement: 'I said: after the abundance (of missed prayers) or not?' Indeed, when he made up the month's prayers except one or two farḍ, I said the missed prayers are after the abundance, so the original order does not return unless he makes up all. And according to some mashāyikh, if they become few after the abundance, the order returns. Al-Sarakhsī chose the first; the author of al-Muḥīṭ said: and upon it is the fatwa. 'He prayed five while remembering a missed prayer, so the five are invalid in a suspended manner.' And Abū Ḥafṣ al-Kabīr inclined to permissibility, and it was chosen by Fakhr al-Islām, Shams al-Aʾimmah, the author of al-Muḥīṭ, Qāḍī Khān, and others. Its reasoning is that when the order (tartīb) falls, the fallen does not return, like impure water entering flowing water such that it increases and flows, then returns to a small amount, it does not become impure. His statement: al-Sarakhsī: a relative attribution to Sarakhs... His statement: the author of al-Muḥīṭ... His statement: 'remembering' is a condition because if he did not remember, the obligation of order falls due to forgetfulness, and if he remembered in some and forgot in some, it is considered in that which he remembered, and if it reaches five, they are valid, and no attention is given to that which he forgot. His statement: 'the five are invalid in a suspended manner': its clarification: when a prayer (even witr) is missed, then whenever he prays an on-time prayer after it while remembering that (missed prayer), the on-time prayer becomes invalid with a suspended invalidity dependent on making up that missed prayer. If he performs a sixth, all become valid; if he makes up the missed prayer, the farḍiyyah of the five becomes nullified, not their essence. 'If he performs a sixth, all become valid; if he makes up the missed prayer, the farḍiyyah of the five becomes nullified, not their essence.' A man missed a prayer, then prayed five after it while remembering it; these five become invalid due to the obligation of order. However, according to Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad (may Allāh have mercy on them), the invalidity is not suspended, and that is qiyās. According to Abū Ḥanīfa, it is a suspended invalidity: if he performs a sixth, all become valid; if he makes up the missed prayer, then the five he performed have their description of farḍiyyah nullified. Indeed, nullification of farḍiyyah does not necessitate nullification of the prayer according to Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf, unlike Muḥammad. If he makes it up before praying five prayers after it, the invalidity becomes definitive, and what he prayed before making up the missed prayer from on-time prayers becomes nafl, and their description of farḍiyyah is nullified. If he does not make it up until the time of the fifth (prayer) exits, and the invalid prayers together with the missed one become six, they all become valid. His statement: 'if he performs a sixth': its apparent meaning is that the validity of all is dependent on performing six prayers after the missed one, and that is mentioned in al-Hidāyah, al-Kāfī, and most books. It was claimed in al-Baḥr that this is wrong. Ibn al-Humām mentioned in al-Fatḥ that validity is dependent on the entry of the time of the sixth. This was refuted in al-Nahr that the entry of the time of the sixth after the missed one is not a condition; the truth is what is mentioned in Miʿrāj al-Dirāyah, Imdād al-Fattāḥ, al-Tātārkhāniyyah, and others: what is considered is the exit of the time of the fifth because by that the missed prayers become six, and what is in most books regarding considering the performance of the sixth is only so that the missed prayers become six with certainty, not that it is a condition at all. His statement: 'and that is qiyās' because the abrogator of order is only the abundance before performing a prayer, not the abundance occurring after it. So if he prays a prayer while remembering a missed one, it becomes invalid immediately and definitively due to the non-occurrence of the abundance of missed prayers that abrogates the order, regardless of whether the abundance occurs after or not. His statement: 'he made up the missed prayer' means before performing the sixth, indeed before the exit of the time of the fifth. His statement: 'contrary to Muḥammad' because the taḥrīmah was contracted for the farḍ, not for other than it, so if the description of farḍiyyah—which is the intended purpose of the taḥrīmah—is nullified, the taḥrīmah is nullified. And for both of them (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf): indeed the taḥrīmah is contracted for the prayer along with the description of farḍiyyah, and it is not necessary that...
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling of praying five prayers while remembering a missed obligatory prayer. The text begins by stating the ruling: if a person prays five prayers (in order, like the five daily prayers) while being mindful of a missed prayer, those five are invalid in a suspended manner (mawqūfan). This means their validity depends on a future action. The commentary explains that this is a subdivision of the earlier discussion on whether the order (tartīb) is re-established after many prayers have been missed. Some mashāyikh hold that if the missed prayers become few (less than five), the order returns; others hold it does not. Al-Sarakhsī chose the first opinion, and fatwa is given accordingly. The text then presents a minority view from Abū Ḥafṣ al-Kabīr and others that the five prayers are not invalid at all, analogizing to water that becomes pure after a large quantity of pure water flows into it. However, the main dispute is between Imām Abū Ḥanīfa and the two disciples, Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad al-Shaybānī. According to Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad, the five prayers become invalid immediately and definitively (fasād bātt) because the order of prayers is still obligatory; the abundance that abrogates the order only counts if it occurs before any prayer is performed. According to Abū Ḥanīfa, the invalidity is suspended: if the person performs a sixth prayer (which makes the missed prayers six, thus abrogating the order), the five become valid; if instead he makes up the missed prayer first, then the five become nafl (voluntary) and lose their farḍiyyah (obligatory description) but remain valid as prayers. The text clarifies a further difference: Imām Muḥammad holds that if the farḍiyyah is nullified, the entire prayer becomes invalid because the taḥrīmah (initial takbīr) was intended for a farḍ prayer; Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf hold that the prayer itself remains valid as nafl because the taḥrīmah served for the prayer, not solely for its description. The passage also discusses what exactly triggers the reversal: the majority say performing a sixth prayer; some later scholars say exiting the time of the fifth prayer is sufficient, as that makes the missed prayers six in number. The muftā bihī (relied-upon position) is that of Abū Ḥanīfa, with the sixth prayer being a clear indicator, though the condition is actually the exit of the fifth prayer's time.

Key Terms
موقوفاً (mawqūfan): Suspended invalidity; a ruling whose final effect depends on a future action (e.g., making up the missed prayer or praying a sixth).
الترتيب (al-tartīb): The obligation to pray missed prayers in order, and to pray on-time prayers after missed ones in order.
التحريمة (al-taḥrīmah): The initial takbīr (Allāhu Akbar) that commences the prayer and makes worldly actions forbidden during it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), what happens if a person, while remembering a missed fajr prayer, prays five complete prayers (zuhr, asr, maghrib, isha, and the next fajr)?
A) All five prayers are invalid and must be repeated.
B) The five prayers are invalid in a suspended manner; if he then prays the sixth prayer (zuhr of the next day), all five become valid.
C) The five prayers are valid because the missed prayer is only one.
D) Only the first prayer is invalid; the rest are valid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on Abu Hanifa's view that five prayers prayed after a missed one are invalid in a suspended manner; a sixth prayer can restore their validity.

Salah — Prayer
advanced
10 min
The Suspended Invalidity: Abu Hanifa's View on Order of Missed Prayers
Intra-Hanafi dispute on suspended vs. immediate invalidity for prayers without order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between suspended (muwaqqaf) and immediate (bat) invalidity in the Hanafi school regarding prayers performed out of order when a single prayer is missed, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah) of this difference.

Core Idea
A missing prayer can be made up later, but prayers performed out of order may become valid if the number of missed prayers later reaches six.

This passage dives into a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute. When a person misses one prayer and then prays five subsequent prayers while remembering it, those five are considered invalid due to the obligation of order. But are they immediately and irrevocably invalid, or could they become valid later? The answer reveals a deeper principle about how rulings can be contingent on future events.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the shared ruling
Note the scenario: a person misses one prayer (faa'ita) and, while remembering it, performs five subsequent prayers. All Hanafi imams agree these five are invalid due to the obligation of tarteeb (order). What is contested is the type and consequence of this invalidity.
The shared ruling is 'fasad' (invalidity), but its nature is disputed.
2
State the Sahibayn's position and its proof (qiyas)
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that each invalid prayer is immediately and irrevocably invalid (fasad bat). Their proof: The factor that removes the obligation of order is a large number of missed prayers (kathrah) existing before performing the prayer. Since only one prayer is missed initially, the obligation of order remains, so each prayer is invalid from its start.
Qiyas: They compare to the general rule that order is obligatory unless the missed prayers are numerous.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position and its proof (istihsan)
Abu Hanifa holds that the invalidity is suspended (fasad muwaqqaf). If the person later performs a sixth prayer, all five become valid. If he instead makes up the missed prayer first, the five become nafl (supererogatory) but remain valid as prayers. His proof is istihsan: The quantity that removes order can be considered present in the entire set of prayers, not just at the moment of each prayer. Rulings can be contingent on future events, as seen in advance zakat and Friday Zuhr.
Istihsan: A more equitable ruling overrides strict qiyas.
4
Examine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In this dispute, the practical consequence appears when the person laughs during one of the five prayers before resolution. According to the Sahibayn, since the prayer is already invalid, laughing nullifies wudu. According to Abu Hanifa, since the prayer may later become valid, laughing does not nullify wudu.
This thamarah shows how the difference in the nature of invalidity affects ancillary rulings.
5
Understand the later clarification on the condition for validity
Many early texts state that validity depends on performing a sixth prayer. However, later scholars (e.g., Ibn al-Humam) clarify that the true condition is the exit of the time of the fifth prayer, because that makes the number of missed prayers reach six. Performing the sixth is only a means to be certain.
This refinement shows the importance of distinguishing between condition (shart) and certainty (yaqin).

Book of Prayer / Making Up Missed Prayers: If he performs a sixth (prayer), all are valid; if he makes up the missed prayer, the prescribed nature of the five is invalidated, not their essence. A man who missed a prayer, then performed five prayers while remembering it, those five are corrupted due to the obligation of order. However, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them), it is an unsuspended corruption, and that is the analogy. According to Abu Hanifa, it is a suspended corruption: if he performs a sixth, all are valid; if he makes up the missed prayer, then the five he performed lose the attribute of being obligatory, for the invalidation of the attribute does not necessitate the invalidation of the prayer according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. If he makes it up before he prays five prayers after it, the corruption becomes irrevocable, and what he prayed before making up the missed prayer becomes supererogatory from the time-based prayers, and their attribute of obligation is nullified. If he does not make it up until the time of the fifth expires, and the corrupted prayers together with the missed one become six, all become valid. His statement: 'If he performs a sixth' – its apparent meaning is that the validity of all is contingent upon performing six prayers after the omitted one, and that is mentioned in al-Hidayah, al-Kafi, and most books. However, it was claimed in al-Bahr that this is an error. Ibn al-Humam mentioned in al-Fath that validity is contingent upon the entry of the time of the sixth. This was refuted in al-Nahr: the entry of the time of the sixth after the omitted one is not a condition. The correct view is what is mentioned in Miraj al-Dirayah, Imdad al-Fattah, al-Tatarkhaniyyah, and others: what is considered is the exit of the time of the fifth, because through that the missed prayers become six. What is in most books about considering the performance of the sixth is only to ensure that the missed prayers become six with certainty, not that it is a condition at all. His statement: 'and that is the analogy' – because the remover of the order is only the quantity before performing a prayer, not the quantity that occurs after it. So if he prays a prayer while remembering a missed one, it becomes corrupted immediately with irrevocable corruption, due to the non-existence of a quantity of missed prayers that removes the order, regardless of whether the quantity occurs afterwards or not. His statement: 'makes up the missed prayer' – i.e., before performing the sixth, rather before the exit of the time of the fifth. His statement: 'contrary to Muhammad' – (Muhammad's argument:) The takbir al-tahrimah was contracted for the obligatory prayer, not for other than it; so if the attribute of obligation is invalidated, which is the purpose of the tahrimah, the tahrimah is invalidated. And for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): The tahrimah was contracted for the prayer with the attribute of obligation, and it is not necessary that the invalidation of the attribute implies the invalidation of the essence. The practical difference appears in the case where he laughs out loud before exiting the prayer: his ablution is nullified according to them, not according to him (Abu Hanifa). Thus in al-Hidayah and al-Binayah. His statement: 'And he only said... etc.' – Al-Fath al-Qadir says: 'The rationale of his statement (Abu Hanifa's), which is istihsan: The remover is quantity, and it is present in all; so it must bring about removal. For this reason, if he repeats them without order, they are valid according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) as well. This is because the impediment to validity is fewness (of missed prayers), and it has been removed; so the impediment is lifted. And it is not impossible for a ruling to be contingent on a matter until its state becomes clear, like the advance payment of zakat to a poor person: its being obligatory is contingent upon completion of the year and the nisab; if the year is completed, it becomes obligatory, otherwise supererogatory; and like the Maghrib prayer on the way to Muzdalifah being obligatory contingent on not repeating it before dawn; if he repeats it, it becomes supererogatory; and the Zuhr prayer on Friday contingent on not attending the Friday prayer; if he attends, it becomes supererogatory.' His statement: 'when... etc.' – i.e., when he prays the sixth, it becomes clear that the observance of order was directed towards the many, because the missed prayers have then reached the limit of quantity.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the ruling of performing prayers out of order when a single prayer has been missed. The scenario: a person misses one prayer (faa'ita) and then, while remembering it, performs five subsequent prayers. All agree that these five prayers are invalid due to the obligation of tarteeb (order). However, they differ on the nature of this invalidity and its consequences. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that each of the five prayers is immediately and irrevocably invalid (fasad bat). Their proof is qiyas: the factor that removes the obligation of order is a large number (three or more) of missed prayers existing before performing any prayer. Since there is only one missed prayer at the time of performing the five, the obligation of order remains, so each prayer is invalid as soon as it is performed. Abu Hanifa holds that the invalidity is suspended (fasad muwaqqaf). If the person later performs a sixth prayer (making the total missed prayers six, which is the threshold for removing order), then all five become valid retroactively. If instead he makes up the original missed prayer before performing a sixth, then the five become nafl (supererogatory) but still valid as prayers, losing only the attribute of obligation. The key difference is that Abu Hanifa considers the potential for the missed prayers to become numerous (kathrah) after the fact, while the Sahibayn consider only the existing number at the time of each prayer. The text also clarifies a common misunderstanding: many books state that validity depends on actually performing a sixth prayer, but later scholars (like Ibn al-Humam and others) clarify that the condition is simply the exit of the time of the fifth prayer, because at that point the missed prayers have become six (the original missed one plus the five performed). The practical consequence (thamarah) of this difference appears in a case where the person laughs during one of the five prayers before the situation is resolved: according to Abu Hanifa, since the prayer may later become valid or nafl, laughing does not nullify wudu; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, since the prayer is already irrevocably invalid, laughing nullifies wudu. The author also supports Abu Hanifa's view by citing istihsan: just as advance zakat or Friday Zuhr are contingent on later conditions, so the validity of these prayers can be suspended.

Key Terms
فائتة (fā'ita): A single missed obligatory prayer that a person is obligated to make up in order.
فساد موقوف (fasād muwaqqaf): A type of invalidity that is suspended pending a future event; the prayer is considered defective but its final ruling depends on later actions.
فساد بات (fasād bāt): Irrevocable and immediate invalidity; the prayer is invalid from its start and cannot become valid later.
ترتيب (tartīb): The obligation to perform prayers in their prescribed chronological order, especially when making up missed prayers.
كثرة الفوائت (kathrat al-fawā'it): The condition of having many missed prayers (six or more) that removes the obligation of order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the practical difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the prayers performed out of order when a person laughs during one of them?
A) According to all, laughing renders wudu null and the prayer invalid.
B) According to Abu Hanifa, laughing does not nullify wudu; according to the Sahibayn, it does.
C) According to Abu Hanifa, laughing nullifies wudu; according to the Sahibayn, it does not.
D) Laughing has no effect on wudu regardless of the dispute.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's reliance on istihsan allows rulings to be contingent on future events, as seen in Abu Hanifa's view on the suspended invalidity of prayers performed out of order when a single prayer is missed.

intermediate

Suspended Invalidity in Many Missed Prayers: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on suspended invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling of fasād muwaqqaf in the Hanafi school and distinguish the position of Imam Abu Hanifa from the Sahibayn on the validity of missed prayers performed without order.

Core Idea
When missed prayers accumulate to six or more, their status is suspended until the count is clarified — fasād muwaqqaf.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute on how to rule on a set of missed prayers (fawā’it) performed without the required order. Imam Abu Hanifa introduces a unique concept — suspended invalidity (fasād muwaqqaf) — while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, see the prayer as immediately valid. Understanding this ikhtilāf sharpens your grasp of how Hanafi jurists balance textual obligations with practical relief (istihsān).

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa: fasād muwaqqaf) and the Sahibayn position (sahīh immediately). Note that the dispute hinges on whether the cause (illah) of invalidity is the many (kathrah) or the fewness (qillah) of missed prayers.
The passage is from al-Hidayah, a central Hanafi text.
2
Examine the evidence for each side
Abu Hanifa argues that the illah is kathrah, which is present from the start, so invalidity must be suspended. The Sahibayn argue that the prohibition was due to qillah, and once many are reached, the prohibition is lifted, making the prayer valid.
Abu Hanifa supports his view with analogies from zakat, Maghrib at Muzdalifah, and Friday prayer.
3
Analyze the method
Notice that Abu Hanifa uses istihsān and qiyās khafī (hidden analogy) to justify suspension. The Sahibayn rely on the apparent removal of the obstacle (zawāl al-man‘).
The Hanafi school often uses istihsān to achieve practical outcomes.
4
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The concrete difference appears when the worshipper laughs (qahqaha) before exiting the prayer: according to the Sahibayn, his wudu is nullified; according to Abu Hanifa, it is not, because the prayer's status is still suspended.
Laughter nullifies wudu only if the prayer was valid according to the Sahibayn's view.
5
Reflect on authority and application
This dispute is recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah. The mufta bihi position is not specified here; later Hanafi works (e.g., Radd al-Muhtar) may favor the Sahibayn's view for practical ease.
Knowing the authority level helps in issuing fatwa.

(۱) «الهداية» (١) : ٤٩٥). (۲) «البناية» (٢) : ٦٤١). (۳) انتهى من «فتح القدير (٢ : ٤٩٧).
And Abu Hanifa only said: suspended invalidity, because if each one of them is invalid due to observing order, it would be non-suspended invalidity. When he performed the sixth, it became clear that observing order was in the many, and this is false. So we said: suspension until it becomes apparent: if observing order was in the many, it is not permissible; if in the few, it is permissible. The invalidity of the description is the invalidity of the original. The benefit of the disagreement appears if he laughs before exiting the prayer: his purity is nullified according to them (Sahibayn), not according to him (Abu Hanifa). Thus in al-Hidayah and al-Binayah. [1] His saying: 'And only said...' etc.; he said in Fath al-Qadir: 'The basis of his saying—and it is istihsan—is that the cause of vitiation is many (prayers), and it is established in the whole, so it must effect vitiation. Hence if he repeats them without order, it is permissible according to them as well, because the prohibition was due to fewness (of missed prayers), and it has been removed, so the prohibition is removed. It is not impossible for a ruling to be suspended pending clarification, like the acceleration of zakat to a poor person: its being obligatory is suspended upon completion of the year and the sufficiency of the nisab; if completed it becomes obligatory, otherwise it is supererogatory. And the Maghrib prayer at Muzdalifah is obligatory upon not repeating it before dawn; if he repeats it, it becomes supererogatory. And the Zuhr prayer on Friday is obligatory upon not attending (Jumu'ah); if he attends, it becomes supererogatory.' [2] His saying: 'then when he said the sixth...' i.e., when he prayed the sixth, it became apparent that observance of order was pushed into the many because the missed prayers then reached the limit of many.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on performing missed prayers (fawa'it) without order when they accumulate in large numbers. The issue arises when a person has many missed prayers, and then performs them without the required sequential order. Abu Hanifa holds that such prayers are in a state of suspended invalidity (fasid muwaqqaf): if the number of missed prayers is considered 'many' (kathirah), the prayer is invalid; if 'few' (qalilah), it is valid. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the prayer is valid immediately, because the prohibition only applied when they were few, and once they become many, the prohibition is lifted. The passage cites an istihsan argument from Fath al-Qadir: the cause (ills) of invalidity is the large number, which is present in the whole set, so it should effect invalidity. However, the Sahibayn argue that if he repeats them even without order, it is permissible because the obstacle (fewness) has been removed. The passage also gives practical examples from other areas of fiqh to illustrate suspended rulings, such as zakat paid early, Maghrib at Muzdalifah, and Zuhr on Friday. The benefit of this khilaf is demonstrated in the case of laughing (qahqaha) before exiting the prayer: according to Abu Hanifa, the purity is not nullified, while according to the Sahibayn, it is nullified.

Key Terms
فساد موقوف (fasād muwaqqaf): Suspended invalidity: a legal status where the invalidity of an act is contingent upon a future clarification (here, whether the number of missed prayers is many or few).
كثرة (kathrah): Many (missed prayers): the specific threshold beyond which the ruling changes. In this context, it is defined as six or more missed prayers.
قلة (qillah): Few (missed prayers): fewer than six, where the order requirement strictly applies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the status of a prayer performed without order when the total missed prayers reach six?
A) It is valid immediately.
B) It is invalid immediately.
C) It is in a state of suspended invalidity (fasid muwaqqaf).
D) It is makruh but valid.

Key Takeaway
When a person has many missed prayers (six or more), performing them without order leads to a ruling of fasād muwaqqaf according to Imam Abu Hanifa, with the Sahibayn holding immediate validity — a dispute with practical consequences for wudu nullification.

Salah — Prayer

The Ruling of Sujud al-Sahw: Obligation and Conditions
Definition and ruling of sujud al-sahw with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling of Sujud al-Sahw, identify its purpose as compensation for a deficiency, and derive the conditions under which it becomes obligatory.

Core Idea
Sujud al-Sahw is wajib only to compensate for omitting a wajib, not for sunan or rukn.

When we make a mistake in prayer, many of us instinctively perform Sujud al-Sahw. But is it always required? The Hanafi school teaches that Sujud al-Sahw is not for every slip—it is specifically for a deficiency caused by omitting a wajib. This lesson unpacks the ruling from the classical text, the evidence behind it, and the precise conditions that trigger its obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
First, locate the act being discussed: Sujud al-Sahw (two prostrations after the salam). The text states that the correct and chosen view is that it is wajib (obligatory), not sunnah as al-Quduri held.
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: al-Quduri's view is sunnah, but the majority (and the author) say wajib.
2
Understand the Legal Basis (Dalil)
The text cites the hadith: 'For every forgetfulness, there are two prostrations after the salam.' This command indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul. Additionally, the Prophet's continuous practice (muwazabah) reinforces the obligation.
The dalil is a hadith ahad, so its thubut is zanni, but its dalala is also zanni; the Hanafis still treat it as wajib due to the command and practice.
3
Analyze the Purpose: Jabr (Compensation)
The text explains that Sujud al-Sahw is wajib because it compensates (jabr) for a deficiency (nuqsan) that has occurred in the prayer. This is analogous to dam in Hajj—both are wajib compensations.
The deficiency is specifically the omission of a wajib, not a sunnah or a rukn.
4
Distinguish What Triggers Sujud al-Sahw
From the principle, the text deduces that Sujud al-Sahw is required only when a wajib is omitted (or similar, like delaying a wajib). It is not required for omitting sunan (like ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, thana') because those are not obligatory in themselves. Nor is it required for omitting a rukn, whether intentionally or forgetfully, because a rukn omission invalidates the prayer entirely.
Key distinction: rukn → prayer invalid (batil); wajib omitted → prayer valid but sujud required; sunnah omitted → no sujud.
5
Note the Timing and Intentional Omission
The text states that Sujud al-Sahw is performed after the salam (based on the hadith). If a wajib is omitted intentionally, however, Sujud al-Sahw is not sufficient—the prayer must be repeated because the hadiths mention sujud for forgetfulness, not for intentional acts.
This reinforces that Sujud al-Sahw is specifically for forgetful omission of a wajib.

Chapter on Sujud al-Sahw
[1] His saying: Chapter on Sujud al-Sahw; i.e., this is a chapter explaining the rulings of the two prostrations of forgetfulness. Sujud (with damm on sad) is a verbal noun, and it applies to both few and many (prostrations). Its idafah (construct) is of the type of adding something to its cause.
Sunnah,
(1)
(2) His saying: 'It is obligatory for him'; i.e., for the worshipper. This is the correct chosen view, and al-Quduri mentioned that it is sunnah. The basis of the correct view: it is compensation for a deficiency that occurred in the prayer, so it is obligatory like the blood (dama) in Hajj. The command to do it and the Prophet's (peace be upon him) constant practice, which is established in the Six Authentic Books and others, testify to it. Thus in al-Bahr and others. When its obligation is established, it is established that it is only obligatory due to a deficiency caused by omitting a wajib or similar, not by omitting sunan or other things. So it is not obligatory for omitting the ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, thana', etc., because something that is not obligatory in itself cannot be compensated for a deficiency occurring in it. And it is also not obligatory for omitting a rukn, whether intentionally or forgetfully, because omitting a rukn invalidates the prayer outright, and deficiency established by it cannot be compensated. If one intentionally omits a wajib, he does prostrate, because what is transmitted in the hadiths is prostration for forgetfulness, not for intentionality—rather, he repeats the prayer.
(r)
[3] His saying: 'after the salam'; due to the hadith: 'For every forgetfulness, there are two prostrations after the salam.' Ahmad, Ibn Majah, and Abu Dawud narrated it. And it is established that he (peace be upon him) prostrated the two prostrations of forgetfulness after the salam, narrated by al-Bukhari and Muslim and others. It is also narrated from the Six Imams that he prostrated before the salam, and by it...
(1) al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (2:99).
(2) In Musnad Ahmad (5:281), Sunan Abi Dawud (1:339), Musnad al-Ruyani (2:265), Ma'rifat al-Sunan wa al-Athar (3:440), al-Mu'jam al-Kabir (2:92), and others.
(3) From Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him): 'When one of you is in doubt in his prayer, let him seek the correct (estimate) and complete it, then say salam, then prostrate two prostrations.' In Sahih al-Bukhari (1:156), Sahih Muslim (1:400), and others.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on Sujud al-Sahw (Prostration of Forgetfulness). The commentator first defines the chapter title: 'Sujud al-Sahw' is a prostration added to the prayer to compensate for a deficiency caused by forgetfulness. The word 'sujud' is a verbal noun (masdar) and applies to both a single prostration and multiple, though here it specifically means two prostrations. The idafah (construct phrase) indicates that forgetfulness (sahw) is the cause of this prostration.

The commentator then addresses the ruling: 'It is obligatory for him (the worshipper).' He clarifies that the correct and chosen view is that Sujud al-Sahw is wajib (obligatory). Imam al-Quduri is reported to have held that it is sunnah, but the majority (including the author) hold it as wajib. The reasoning is that it compensates for a deficiency (nuqsan) that has occurred in the prayer, analogous to the dam (blood offering) in Hajj which is wajib for certain violations. The obligation is supported by the Prophet's (peace be upon him) command and continuous practice as recorded in authentic hadith collections.

From the obligation of Sujud al-Sahw, it follows that it is only required when a deficiency occurs due to omitting a wajib (obligatory part) or something similar. It is not required for omitting sunnah acts (like ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, thana') because those are not obligatory in themselves. Nor is it required for omitting a rukn (pillar), whether intentionally or forgetfully, because omitting a rukn invalidates the prayer entirely—it cannot be compensated; the prayer must be repeated. If a wajib is omitted intentionally, Sujud al-Sahw is not sufficient; the prayer must be repeated because the hadiths mention sujud for forgetfulness, not for intentional omission.

Finally, the commentator discusses when the prostration is performed: after the salam. He cites the hadith 'For every forgetfulness, there are two prostrations after the salam' and notes that the Prophet (peace be upon him) is authentically reported to have prostrated after salam (in Sahihayn) and also before salam (in other authentic reports). The text appears to be a heading for a more detailed discussion.

Key Terms
سجود السهو (sujūd al-sahw): Two prostrations performed to compensate for a deficiency (nuqsan) caused by forgetfulness in prayer, typically after the salam.
جبر (jabr): Compensation: Sujud al-Sahw acts as a compensatory act to remedy a deficiency in the prayer without invalidating it.
نقصان (nuqsān): Deficiency: the omission of a wajib (obligatory element) in prayer that triggers the need for Sujud al-Sahw.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view in this passage, when is Sujud al-Sahw obligatory?
A) For omitting any sunnah act in prayer.
B) For omitting a rukn (pillar) forgetfully.
C) For omitting a wajib act in prayer.
D) For any forgetful action, regardless of type.

Key Takeaway
Sujud al-Sahw is a wajib compensation for the forgetful omission of a wajib in prayer; it serves to perfect the prayer without invalidating it.

The Ruling of Sujud al-Sahw: Wajib and Its Proof
Ruling and proof of sujud al-sahw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that sujud al-sahw is wajib, cite the primary hadith and qiyas as proof, and identify which omissions trigger it (forgetful omission of a wajib, not sunnah or rukn).

Core Idea
Sujud al-sahw is wajib only for forgetful omission of a wajib, compensating like dam in Hajj.

This lesson establishes the ruling of sujud al-sahw—whether it is wajib or sunnah—and clarifies its scope. The correct Hanafi position, supported by hadith and qiyas, makes it wajib when a wajib is omitted forgetfully. We also examine the intra-Hanafi difference with al-Quduri, who considered it sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sujud al-sahw (two prostrations) performed to compensate for forgetfulness in prayer. Its ruling is stated as wajib (obligatory) in the correct view.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'For every forgetfulness there are two prostrations after the salam' (recorded by Ahmad, Ibn Majah, Abu Dawud) and the Prophet's constant practice.
This is a hadith ahad with zanni thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'sajdatān' indicates the act is prescribed; the imperative implication and the Prophet's continuous practice indicate obligation (wujub). The analogy to dam in Hajj reinforces the wajib status.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub unless overridden.
4
Note the scope: what triggers it
Sujud al-sahw is only wajib for forgetful omission of a wajib or its equivalent. It does not apply for omitting a sunnah (e.g., ta'awwudh) or a rukn (which invalidates prayer entirely).
Also, intentional omission of a wajib requires repetition, not sujud.
5
Address the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Quduri held sujud al-sahw is sunnah, not wajib. The author declares the correct and chosen view is wajib, citing the proof.
The weight of evidence favors wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب سجود السهو
قوله : باب سجود السهو؛ أي هذا باب في بيان أحكام سجدتي السهو، والسجود بالضم مصدر، وهو يطلق على القليل والكثير، وإضافته من قبيل إضافة الشيء إلى سببه.
قوله : يجب له؛ أي للمصلي، هذا هو الصحيح المختار، وذكر القُدوري أنه سنة، وجه الصحيح : أنه جبر لنقصان تمكن في الصلاة، فيجب كالدماء في الحج، ويشهد الأمر به، والمواظبة عليه من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الثابت في كتب الصحاح الستة وغيرها، كذا في «البحر»، وغيره. وإذا ثبت وجوبه ثبت أنه لا يجب إلا بنقصان بترك واجب ونحوه، لا بترك السنن وغيرها، فلا يجب بتركِ التعوّذ والتسمية والثناء وغير ذلك؛ لأنّ ما لا يجب هو بنفسه كيف يجب جبر نقصان واقع به، ولا يجب أيضاً بترك ركن عمداً كان أو سهواً؛ لأنَّ ترك الركن مبطل للصلاة رأساً، ولا يمكن جبر نقصان ثابت به، ولو ترك الواجب عمداً يسجد؛ لأنَّ الوارد في الأحاديث هو السجود عند السهو لا عند العمد، بل يعيد الصلاة.

قوله : وتشهد؛ لأن سجود السهو يرفعُ التشهد السابق، فيتشهد بعده، ويصلي على النبي ويدعو بما شاءَ ثمَّ يُسلّم، يدلّ عليه حديث عمران: «أنّ النبي صلى بهم فسها فسجد سجدتين ثم تشهد ثمّ سلّم»، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي.
وعن أبي هريرة: إن رسول الله سلم، ثم سجد سجدتي السهو، وهو جالس ثم سلم.
Chapter of Sujud al-Sahw. His statement: Chapter of Sujud al-Sahw; meaning this chapter is about explaining the rulings of the two prostrations of forgetfulness, and 'sujud' with a damma on the sad is a verbal noun, and it is used for both few and many, and its addition is of the type of adding a thing to its cause. His statement: It is obligatory for him; meaning for the one praying, this is the correct and chosen view, and al-Quduri mentioned that it is sunnah. The face of the correct view: it is a compensation for a deficiency that occurred in the prayer, so it is obligatory like the blood sacrifices in Hajj, and the command to it and the continuous practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) attested in the six authentic books and others bear witness, as in 'al-Bahr' and others. When its obligation is established, it is established that it is only obligatory due to a deficiency caused by omitting a wajib or the like, not due to omitting the sunan and others. So it is not obligatory due to omitting the ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, thana', and others; because what is not obligatory in itself, how can the compensation for a deficiency occurring through it be obligatory? And it is also not obligatory due to omitting a rukn, intentionally or forgetfully; because omitting a rukn invalidates the prayer completely, and a deficiency established by it cannot be compensated. If one omits a wajib intentionally, he does not prostrate; because what is mentioned in the hadiths is prostration at the time of forgetfulness, not at the time of intentionality; rather, he repeats the prayer. His statement: after salam; due to the hadith: 'For every forgetfulness there are two prostrations after the salam', reported by Ahmad, Ibn Majah, and Abu Dawud, and it is established that he prostrated the two prostrations of forgetfulness after the salam, reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim and others, and it is also narrated from the six imams his prostration before the salam, and the commentator adopted it, and all are permissible according to us and him, and the difference is about precedence, and the truth is that all of that is established as al-'Ayni detailed in 'al-Binayah'. His statement: one; meaning to his right, as al-Karkhi chose, or facing his direction as Fakhr al-Islam chose, and what is corrected in 'al-Hidayah', 'al-Yanabi'', 'al-Zahiriyyah' and others is that it is after the two salams, directing the salam mentioned in the hadiths to what is customary. As in the commentaries of 'al-Munyah'. His statement: and tashahhud; because sujud al-sahw lifts the previous tashahhud, so he recites tashahhud after it, and sends blessings on the Prophet and makes supplication as he wishes, then says salam. This is indicated by the hadith of 'Imran: 'The Prophet led them in prayer and forgot, so he prostrated two prostrations, then recited tashahhud, then said salam', reported by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi. And from Abu Hurairah: The Messenger of Allah said salam, then prostrated two prostrations of forgetfulness while sitting, then said salam.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the chapter heading on Sujud al-Sahw (prostration of forgetfulness) from a Hanafi fiqh commentary. The author begins by defining the term: 'sujud' is a verbal noun covering both few and many prostrations, and the construct 'sujud al-sahw' attributes the act to its cause. He then addresses the ruling: it is wajib (obligatory) in the correct and chosen view, contrary to al-Quduri who considered it sunnah. The reasoning is that sujud al-sahw compensates for a deficiency in prayer, analogous to the obligatory blood sacrifices (dam) in Hajj. Support comes from the Prophet's command and constant practice as recorded in the six major hadith collections. The author clarifies that this obligation only arises from omitting a wajib (necessary act) or something similar, not from omitting a sunnah (such as ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, thana') because what is not obligatory in itself cannot demand compensation. It also does not apply for omitting a rukn (pillar) whether intentionally or forgetfully, since that invalidates the prayer entirely and cannot be fixed by prostration. If one intentionally omits a wajib, no sujud is performed; instead, the prayer must be repeated. Regarding timing, the author cites the hadith 'For every forgetfulness there are two prostrations after the salam' and notes that it is also reported that the Prophet prostrated before salam. The commentator (al-Marghinani) adopted the after-salam view, but all are permissible; the difference is about which is preferred. The truth is that both are established, as detailed by al-'Ayni. Next, the number of salams is discussed: some say one salam to the right (as chosen by al-Karkhi) or facing straight (Fakhr al-Islam), but the corrected view in al-Hidayah and others is two salams, interpreting the 'salam' in hadiths as the customary two salams. Finally, the tashahhud after sujud is required because sujud al-sahw nullifies the previous tashahhud, so one recites tashahhud, sends blessings, makes du'a, then gives salam. Evidence is provided from the hadith of 'Imran and Abu Hurairah.

Key Terms
سجود السهو (sujūd al-sahw): Two prostrations performed to compensate for deficiencies caused by forgetfulness in prayer.
واجب (wājib): A necessary act in prayer that is less than a rukn; its forgetful omission requires sujud al-sahw.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view, when does sujud al-sahw become obligatory?
A) For any forgetfulness in prayer.
B) Only for forgetful omission of a wajib or similar.
C) Only for intentional omission of a sunnah.
D) Only for omission of a rukn.

Key Takeaway
Sujud al-sahw is wajib only for forgetful omission of a wajib in prayer, proven by hadith and qiyas, and it does not compensate for omitted sunan or arkan.

Performance of Sujud al-Sahw: Timing, Salams, and Tashahhud
Performance details of sujud al-sahw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi procedure for sujud al-sahw, including whether it is performed before or after salam, the number of salams, and the requirement of tashahhud.

Core Idea
Sujud al-sahw is performed after two salams with tashahhud, based on Prophetic practice.

This lesson focuses on the practical performance of sujud al-sahw: its timing (after salam or before), the number of salams (one or two), and the tashahhud after sujud. The corrected Hanafi view is that it is done after the two salams, with a tashahhud, and then salam again.

Guided Path
1
Determine the timing of sujud
The text presents two positions: after salam (based on the hadith) and before salam (also reported). The commentator adopted after salam, but both are permissible; the difference is about preference, not obligation.
The truth is both are established.
2
Specify the number of salams
There is a difference: some say one salam to the right, others facing straight. The corrected view in al-Hidayah is two salams, interpreting the 'salam' in hadiths as the customary two salams.
The phrase 'بعد التسليمتين' indicates after the two salams.
3
Explain the tashahhud requirement
Sujud al-sahw nullifies the previous tashahhud, so one must recite tashahhud after sujud, send blessings, make du'a, and then say salam.
Evidence from hadith of 'Imran and Abu Hurairah.

قوله : باب سجود السهو؛ أي هذا باب في بيان أحكام سجدتي السهو، والسجود بالضم مصدر، وهو يطلق على القليل والكثير، وإضافته من قبيل إضافة الشيء إلى سببه.
قوله : يجب له؛ أي للمصلي، هذا هو الصحيح المختار، وذكر القُدوري أنه سنة، وجه الصحيح : أنه جبر لنقصان تمكن في الصلاة، فيجب كالدماء في الحج، ويشهد الأمر به، والمواظبة عليه من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الثابت في كتب الصحاح الستة وغيرها، كذا في «البحر»، وغيره. وإذا ثبت وجوبه ثبت أنه لا يجب إلا بنقصان بترك واجب ونحوه، لا بترك السنن وغيرها، فلا يجب بتركِ التعوّذ والتسمية والثناء وغير ذلك؛ لأنّ ما لا يجب هو بنفسه كيف يجب جبر نقصان واقع به، ولا يجب أيضاً بترك ركن عمداً كان أو سهواً؛ لأنَّ ترك الركن مبطل للصلاة رأساً، ولا يمكن جبر نقصان ثابت به، ولو ترك الواجب عمداً يسجد؛ لأنَّ الوارد في الأحاديث هو السجود عند السهو لا عند العمد، بل يعيد الصلاة.

قوله : وتشهد؛ لأن سجود السهو يرفعُ التشهد السابق، فيتشهد بعده، ويصلي على النبي ويدعو بما شاءَ ثمَّ يُسلّم، يدلّ عليه حديث عمران: «أنّ النبي صلى بهم فسها فسجد سجدتين ثم تشهد ثمّ سلّم»، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي.
وعن أبي هريرة: إن رسول الله سلم، ثم سجد سجدتي السهو، وهو جالس ثم سلم.
Chapter of Sujud al-Sahw. His statement: Chapter of Sujud al-Sahw; meaning this chapter is about explaining the rulings of the two prostrations of forgetfulness, and 'sujud' with a damma on the sad is a verbal noun, and it is used for both few and many, and its addition is of the type of adding a thing to its cause. His statement: It is obligatory for him; meaning for the one praying, this is the correct and chosen view, and al-Quduri mentioned that it is sunnah. The face of the correct view: it is a compensation for a deficiency that occurred in the prayer, so it is obligatory like the blood sacrifices in Hajj, and the command to it and the continuous practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) attested in the six authentic books and others bear witness, as in 'al-Bahr' and others. When its obligation is established, it is established that it is only obligatory due to a deficiency caused by omitting a wajib or the like, not due to omitting the sunan and others. So it is not obligatory due to omitting the ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, thana', and others; because what is not obligatory in itself, how can the compensation for a deficiency occurring through it be obligatory? And it is also not obligatory due to omitting a rukn, intentionally or forgetfully; because omitting a rukn invalidates the prayer completely, and a deficiency established by it cannot be compensated. If one omits a wajib intentionally, he does not prostrate; because what is mentioned in the hadiths is prostration at the time of forgetfulness, not at the time of intentionality; rather, he repeats the prayer. His statement: after salam; due to the hadith: 'For every forgetfulness there are two prostrations after the salam', reported by Ahmad, Ibn Majah, and Abu Dawud, and it is established that he prostrated the two prostrations of forgetfulness after the salam, reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim and others, and it is also narrated from the six imams his prostration before the salam, and the commentator adopted it, and all are permissible according to us and him, and the difference is about precedence, and the truth is that all of that is established as al-'Ayni detailed in 'al-Binayah'. His statement: one; meaning to his right, as al-Karkhi chose, or facing his direction as Fakhr al-Islam chose, and what is corrected in 'al-Hidayah', 'al-Yanabi'', 'al-Zahiriyyah' and others is that it is after the two salams, directing the salam mentioned in the hadiths to what is customary. As in the commentaries of 'al-Munyah'. His statement: and tashahhud; because sujud al-sahw lifts the previous tashahhud, so he recites tashahhud after it, and sends blessings on the Prophet and makes supplication as he wishes, then says salam. This is indicated by the hadith of 'Imran: 'The Prophet led them in prayer and forgot, so he prostrated two prostrations, then recited tashahhud, then said salam', reported by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi. And from Abu Hurairah: The Messenger of Allah said salam, then prostrated two prostrations of forgetfulness while sitting, then said salam.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the chapter heading on Sujud al-Sahw (prostration of forgetfulness) from a Hanafi fiqh commentary. The author begins by defining the term: 'sujud' is a verbal noun covering both few and many prostrations, and the construct 'sujud al-sahw' attributes the act to its cause. He then addresses the ruling: it is wajib (obligatory) in the correct and chosen view, contrary to al-Quduri who considered it sunnah. The reasoning is that sujud al-sahw compensates for a deficiency in prayer, analogous to the obligatory blood sacrifices (dam) in Hajj. Support comes from the Prophet's command and constant practice as recorded in the six major hadith collections. The author clarifies that this obligation only arises from omitting a wajib (necessary act) or something similar, not from omitting a sunnah (such as ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, thana') because what is not obligatory in itself cannot demand compensation. It also does not apply for omitting a rukn (pillar) whether intentionally or forgetfully, since that invalidates the prayer entirely and cannot be fixed by prostration. If one intentionally omits a wajib, no sujud is performed; instead, the prayer must be repeated. Regarding timing, the author cites the hadith 'For every forgetfulness there are two prostrations after the salam' and notes that it is also reported that the Prophet prostrated before salam. The commentator (al-Marghinani) adopted the after-salam view, but all are permissible; the difference is about which is preferred. The truth is that both are established, as detailed by al-'Ayni. Next, the number of salams is discussed: some say one salam to the right (as chosen by al-Karkhi) or facing straight (Fakhr al-Islam), but the corrected view in al-Hidayah and others is two salams, interpreting the 'salam' in hadiths as the customary two salams. Finally, the tashahhud after sujud is required because sujud al-sahw nullifies the previous tashahhud, so one recites tashahhud, sends blessings, makes du'a, then gives salam. Evidence is provided from the hadith of 'Imran and Abu Hurairah.

التشهد (al-tashahhud): The testimony of faith recited while sitting in prayer after the sujud al-sahw.

Components of Sujud al-Sahw and the Ikhtilaf on Salam
States components of sujud al-sahw and presents ikhtilaf on salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three components of sujud al-sahw and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the direction and number of salams, identifying the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Sujud al-sahw requires two prostrations, tashahhud, and salam; the salam's direction differs, but two salams is relied upon.

The matn concisely states the essential components of sujud al-sahw. The commentary then expands on the ikhtilaf regarding the final salam—whether to the right only, both sides, or straight ahead. Understanding these components and the preferred ruling ensures correct performance of this wajib act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
From the matn 'واحد سجدتان وتشهد وسلام', note that sujud al-sahw consists of two prostrations, tashahhud (testimony of faith), and salam (greeting of peace). These are the core elements.
The word 'واحد' (one) refers to the salam direction, not a component.
2
Understand the need for tashahhud
The commentary explains that sujud al-sahw nullifies the previous tashahhud, so it must be recited again after the two prostrations. This is supported by the hadith of 'Imran ibn Husayn.
Without tashahhud, the sujud al-sahw is incomplete; it is a wajib component.
3
Recognize the ikhtilaf on the salam direction
The text lists three opinions: (1) salam to the right only, (2) two salams (right and left), (3) salam straight ahead. Each is attributed to prominent Hanafi scholars.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, all considered valid, but one is preferred.
4
Identify the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view is two salams, as corrected in al-Hidayah, al-Yanabi', and al-Zahiriyyah. This aligns with the general practice of concluding prayers with two salams.
The tarjih is based on the majority of ahadith and the principle of consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحد سجدتان وتشهد وسلام
أخذ الشارح، والكل جائز عندنا وعنده، والخلاف في الأولية، والحق ثبوت ذلك كله كما فصله العيني في «البناية».
قوله : واحد؛ أي عن يمينه، كما اختاره الكرخي، أو تلقاء وجهه كما اختاره فخر الإسلام، والذي صححه في «الهداية» و«الينابيع» و«الظهيرية» وغيرها، هو كونه بعد التسليمتين صرفاً للسلام المذكور في الأحاديث إلى ما هو المعهود. كذا في شروح «المنية».
قد اختلفوا فيه :
الأول : بعد سلام عن يمينه وهو اختيار شيخ الإسلام وفخر الإسلام، وقال في «الكافي» أنه الصواب، وعليه الجمهور، واختاره المصنف، وصاحب «التنوير»، صاحب الدر المختار.
الثاني : بعد تسليمتين، وهو اختيار شمس الأئمة وصدر الإسلام أخي فخر الإسلام، وصححه في «الهداية»، واختاره صاحب «الملتقى».
الثالث : بعد سلام تلقاء وجهه من غير انحراف، وهو مختار فخر الإسلام. كذا في رد المحتار.
قوله : وتشهد؛ لأن سجود السهو يرفع التشهد السابق، فيتشهد بعده، ويصلي على النبي ويدعو بما شاء ثمّ يُسلّم، يدلّ عليه حديث عمران: «أنّ النبي صلى بهم فسها فسجد سجدتين ثم تشهد ثمّ سلّم»، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي.
وعن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه: إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم سلّم، ثم سجد سجدتي السهو، وهو جالس ثم سلّم» في «المجتبى»، و«سنن النسائي الكبرى»، وغيرهما. وعن عمران بن حصين: إن النبي صلى ثلاثاً، ثم سلّم، فقال الخرباق: إنك صليت ثلاثاً فصلي بهم الركعة الباقية ثم سلم، ثم سجد سجدتي السهو، ثم سلم» في «سنن النسائي الكبرى»، و«المجتبى»، وغيرها.
One [sujud al-sahw is] two prostrations, tashahhud, and salam. The commentator [said]: and all are permissible according to us and according to him, and the difference of opinion is regarding the precedence, and the truth is the establishment of all that as al-'Ayni detailed in al-Binayah. His statement: 'one' i.e., to his right, as chosen by al-Karkhi, or facing his face as chosen by Fakhr al-Islam, and what is corrected in al-Hidayah, al-Yanabi', al-Zahiriyyah and others, is that it is after the two salams, as a turning of the salam mentioned in the ahadith to what is customary. Thus in the commentaries of al-Munyah. They differed concerning it: First: after a salam to his right, which is the choice of Shaykh al-Islam and Fakhr al-Islam, and it is said in al-Kafi that it is correct, and the majority are upon it, and the author chose it, and the author of al-Tanwir, the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar. Second: after two salams, which is the choice of Shams al-A'immah and Sadr al-Islam, the brother of Fakhr al-Islam, and it is corrected in al-Hidayah, and the author of al-Multaqa chose it. Third: after a salam facing his face without turning, which is the choice of Fakhr al-Islam. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. His statement: 'and tashahhud' because sujud al-sahw raises the previous tashahhud, so he performs tashahhud after it, and sends blessings upon the Prophet and makes du'a as he wishes, then he salams. That is indicated by the hadith of 'Imran: 'The Prophet led them in prayer and forgot, so he prostrated two prostrations, then he performed tashahhud, then he salamed.' Narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi. And from Abu Hurayrah: 'The Messenger of Allah salamed, then he prostrated two prostrations of forgetfulness while sitting, then he salamed.' [Reported] in al-Mujtaba and Sunan al-Nasa'i al-Kubra and others. And from 'Imran ibn Husayn: 'The Prophet prayed three [rak'ahs], then salamed, so al-Khirbaq said: You prayed three. So he led them in the remaining rak'ah, then salamed, then he prostrated two prostrations of forgetfulness, then salamed.' [Reported] in Sunan al-Nasa'i al-Kubra and al-Mujtaba and others.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage defines the essential components of sujud al-sahw (the prostrations of forgetfulness). The matn states that sujud al-sahw consists of two prostrations (sajdatayn), followed by tashahhud (the testimony of faith recited while sitting), and finally taslim (the greeting of peace to conclude). The commentator notes that all three elements are permissible in the Hanafi school and according to the author (likely Abu Hanifa or the matn author), but the disagreement is about the priority or order. The correct view is that all three are established.

The commentary then clarifies the word 'one' in the matn: it refers to the direction of the salam after sujud al-sahw. Three positions are reported: (1) salam to the right only, chosen by many later Hanafi scholars; (2) two salams (right and left), which is the position corrected in al-Hidayah and preferred by many; (3) salam straight ahead without turning, chosen by Fakhr al-Islam. The relied-upon position is the second: after two salams, because the common practice in ahadith is to turn to the right and left.

Next, the passage explains why tashahhud is needed: sujud al-sahw effectively nullifies the previous tashahhud (the one recited in the last sitting before the forgetfulness occurred), so it must be repeated after the prostrations. After tashahhud, one sends blessings on the Prophet and makes du'a, then says salam. This is supported by the hadith of 'Imran ibn Husayn where the Prophet prostrated, then did tashahhud, then salamed. Additional ahadith from Abu Hurayrah and 'Imran show different situations but confirm the sequence of salam, prostration, and final salam.

Key Terms
سجود السهو (sujud al-sahw): Prostrations performed to compensate for forgetfulness (sahw) in prayer, consisting of two prostrations with tashahhud and salam.
تشهد (tashahhud): The testimony of faith recited while sitting (al-tahiyyat). In sujud al-sahw, it is recited after the two prostrations.
سلام (salam): The greeting of peace that concludes the prayer. In sujud al-sahw, it is performed after tashahhud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because it is a sunnah of prostration
B) Because sujud al-sahw nullifies the previous tashahhud
C) Because the Prophet always recited tashahhud after any prostration
D) Because it is a pillar of sujud al-sahw

Key Takeaway
Sujud al-sahw comprises two prostrations, tashahhud, and salam; the relied-upon practice for salam is two salams (right and left).

Sujud al-Sahw for Adding to the First Tashahhud: The Six Opinions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the threshold for sujūd al-sahw after adding to first tashahhud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi Hanafi position on when adding to the first tashahhud requires sujud al-sahw and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Adding to the first tashahhud may or may not require sujud al-sahw; the mufta bihi requires the addition to reach 'Hamid Majid'.

A common forgetful act in prayer is adding extra words to the first tashahhud, such as blessings on the Prophet. The Hanafi school has a detailed internal debate on how much addition triggers the obligatory sujud al-sahw. This lesson unpacks the six opinions and identifies the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the default rule
Begin by understanding that forgetfully adding to the first tashahhud (e.g., reciting ṣalāt upon the Prophet) is a change (taghyīr) of a wājib. The default is that sujūd al-sahw is required if the addition is significant. The ikhtilāf is over the threshold.
The source mentions the scenario of delaying standing to the third rakʿah by adding to tashahhud.
2
List the six opinions from the text
Go through each opinion: (1) any addition even a letter; (2) amount of 'Allāhumma ṣalli ʿalā Muḥammad'; (3) delay by length of a rukn; (4) addition including 'wa ʿalā āli Muḥammad'; (5) addition reaching 'Ḥamīd Majīd'; (6) no sujūd at all for adding ṣalāt. Note which scholars support each.
Opinions 3 and 2 are seen as reconcilable by Ibn ʿĀbidīn.
3
Identify the muftā bihī (operative ruling)
The text states 'wa ẓ-ẓāhir annahu lā yajibu mā lam yablugh ilā qawlihi: Ḥamīd Majīd' and cites Radd al-Muḥtār. This is the fifth opinion: the addition must reach the full closing phrase of the ṣalāt to necessitate sujūd al-sahw.
This is the view relied upon for fatwa according to the later authorities (mutākhkhirīn).
4
Explain the tarjīḥ mechanism
Understand that the earlier opinions (e.g., from Abū Ḥanīfa) are not rejected but are seen as less practical. Later scholars harmonized them by focusing on when the addition becomes a separate act. The threshold of 'Ḥamīd Majīd' ensures only a substantial addition triggers compensation.
Ibn ʿĀbidīn's statement 'lā tanāfī' between opinions 2 and 3 shows reconciliation.
5
Apply to a concrete scenario
Consider a person who forgetfully adds 'Allāhumma ṣalli ʿalā Muḥammad' only. According to the muftā bihī, this is less than 'Ḥamīd Majīd' and does not require sujūd al-sahw. If he adds 'wa ʿalā āli Muḥammad Ḥamīd Majīd', sujūd is wajib.
This practical difference shows the importance of the chosen threshold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا قدم ركناً، أو أخره، أو كرره، أو غير واجباً، أو تركه ساهياً: كركوع قبل القراءة، وتأخير القيام إلى الثالثة بزيادة على التشهد.
قوله : قدم... الخ؛ أي قدم ركناً من أركان الصلاة على ما بعده أو أخره عن موضعه.
قوله : بزيادة ... الخ؛ بقراءة الصلاة على النبي أو الأدعية.
قوله : يجب عليه سجود السهو؛ اختلفوا فيه، فقيل : بوجوبه بمطلق الزيادة، ولو بحرف، وقيل : بمقدار اللهم صلي على محمد، وصححه الزيلعي في «شرح الكنز»، واختاره في «البحر» تبعاً لصاحب «الخلاصة» و«الخانية»، والظاهر أنه لا يجب ما لم يبلغ إلى قوله: حميد مجيد. كذا في «رد المحتار».
وتفصيل هذا الاختلاف فيما يجب على أقوال :
الأول : يجب بمطلق الزيادة ولو بحرف، وهو مروي عن أبي حنيفة.
الثاني : يجب بمقدار اللهم صل على محمد، وصححه صاحب التبيين وفتح باب العناية.
الثالث : يجب بالتأخير بمقدار ركن، واختاره صاحب التنوير والدر المنتقى، وصححه صاحب درر الحكام ومجمع الأنهر، قال ابن عابدين في رد المحتار: الظاهر أنه لا تنافي بين هذا القول والقول الثاني.
الرابع : لا يجب ما لم يقل وعلى آل محمد، قال الحلبي في شرح المنية الصغير: هو الأصح، وهو قول الأكثر.
الخامس : لا يجب ما لم يبلغ إلى قوله : حميد مجيد، ينظر التاتارخانية عن الحاوي.
السادس : لا سهو عندهما عليه أصلاً، ففي الزاهدي: وبه أفتى بعض أهل زماننا، وفي المحيط: واستقبح محمد السهو لأجل الصلاة عليه صلى الله عليه وسلم.
If he brings forward a pillar, or delays it, or repeats it, or changes a wajib, or leaves it forgetfully: such as bowing before recitation, and delaying the standing to the third [rak'ah] with an addition to the tashahhud. His statement: 'brings forward... etc.' i.e., he brings forward a pillar of the prayer from its place or delays it from its place. His statement: 'with an addition... etc.' i.e., by reciting blessings on the Prophet or supplications. His statement: 'sujud al-sahw is obligatory upon him'; they differed concerning it: it is said that it is obligatory with any addition, even one letter; and it is said [obligatory] with the amount of 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad', and al-Zayla'i corrected this in Sharh al-Kanz, and he chose it in al-Bahr following the author of al-Khulasah and al-Khaniyyah; and the apparent [view] is that it is not obligatory unless it reaches to his saying: 'Hamid Majid'. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. And the detail of this difference in what is obligatory is on [several] statements: First: it is obligatory with any addition, even a letter, and it is narrated from Abu Hanifa. Second: it is obligatory with the amount of 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad', and the author of al-Tabyin and Fath Bab al-'Inayah corrected it. Third: it is obligatory with the delay by the amount of a pillar, and the author of al-Tanwir and al-Durr al-Muntaqa chose it, and the author of Durar al-Hukkam and Majma' al-Anhur corrected it; Ibn 'Abidin said in Radd al-Muhtar: apparently there is no contradiction between this statement and the second statement. Fourth: it is not obligatory unless he says 'wa 'ala ali Muhammad', al-Halabi said in Sharh al-Munyah al-Saghir: it is the soundest, and it is the statement of the majority. Fifth: it is not obligatory unless he reaches to his saying: 'Hamid Majid'; see al-Tatarkhaniyyah from al-Hawi. Sixth: there is no sahw according to them at all; in al-Zahidi: and some of our time gave fatwa with it, and in al-Muhit: Muhammad considered sahw for the sake of blessings on him disliked.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage addresses a common scenario in prayer: when a person delays the standing to the third rak'ah by adding to the first tashahhud (e.g., reciting extra phrases like blessings on the Prophet). The matn states that such an addition, done forgetfully, triggers sujud al-sahw. The commentary then delves into the intra-Hanafi dispute on the threshold of addition that necessitates sujud al-sahw.

The six opinions are:
1. Any addition, even a single letter, requires sujud al-sahw (narrated from Abu Hanifa).
2. Only if the addition amounts to 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad' (corrected by al-Zayla'i and chosen by al-Bahr).
3. Only if the addition delays the prayer by the length of a pillar (e.g., the time to perform one rukn), and many later scholars agree; Ibn 'Abidin notes this does not contradict opinion 2.
4. Only if the addition includes 'wa 'ala ali Muhammad' (majority opinion according to al-Halabi).
5. Only if the addition reaches 'Hamid Majid' (the end of full blessing).
6. No sahw at all for adding blessings; Imam Muhammad considered it disliked to perform sahw for the sake of sending blessings.

The text then indicates that the apparent (zahir) view is the fifth: sujud al-sahw is not required unless the addition reaches the phrase 'Hamid Majid'. This is cited from Radd al-Muhtar, indicating it is the mufta bihi position. The detailed list of opinions shows the careful calibration of what constitutes a significant addition that disrupts the prayer.

Key Terms
زيادة على التشهد (ziyadat 'ala al-tashahhud): Adding extra words to the obligatory recitation of tashahhud in the first sitting, such as blessings on the Prophet.
سجود السهو (sujud al-sahw): The prostrations of forgetfulness performed to compensate for errors in prayer, including additions to tashahhud.
حميد مجيد (hamid majid): The final two words of the complete salutation upon the Prophet (Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad wa 'ala ali Muhammad kama sallayta 'ala Ibrahima wa 'ala ali Ibrahima innaka Hamidun Majid).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) view in the Hanafi school, when does adding to the first tashahhud obligate sujud al-sahw?
A) Any addition, even one letter
B) When the addition reaches 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad'
C) When the addition reaches 'Hamid Majid' (the end of the full blessing)
D) It never obligates sujud al-sahw

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that sujud al-sahw is not required for adding to the first tashahhud unless the addition reaches the full closing phrase 'Hamid Majid'.

Dispute on Additional Recitation in Tashahhud Necessitating Sujud al-Sahw
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sujud al-sahw due to extra recitation in tashahhud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the threshold for extra recitation in the first tashahhud that necessitates sujud al-sahw, identify the most correct view (mufta bihi) according to later authorities, and apply the ruling to determine whether sujud al-sahw is required in a given case.

Core Idea
Sujud al-sahw is not required for a small addition; the majority view requires the complete salawat including the family.

This passage explores a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the minimal amount of additional recitation in the first tashahhud that triggers the obligation of sujud al-sahw. While Imam Abu Hanifa reportedly said that even a single letter suffices, later scholars refined this, and the preferred position is that a complete salutation on the Prophet and his family ('wa 'ala aali Muhammad') is necessary. Understanding this dispute helps in correctly applying the rules of sujud al-sahw in daily prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act that triggers the dispute
The base text mentions 'delay of standing to the third rak'ah by an addition to the tashahhud.' Recognize that this addition is the basis for the dispute.
The addition is forgetful, not deliberate.
2
State the initial narration from Abu Hanifa
According to the text, Abu Hanifa is reported to have said that any single letter added necessitates sahw.
This is the strictest view.
3
Outline the three main intra-Hanafi positions
List positions: (1) any addition, even a letter; (2) addition of the amount of 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad'; (3) addition of 'wa 'ala aali Muhammad' (complete salawat). Note that al-Zayla'i and others preferred the second, while al-Halabi declared the third as the most correct and the majority view.
The amount of a full pillar is a weaker view.
4
Identify the tarjih and mufta bihi
Al-Halabi's view (requiring 'wa 'ala aali Muhammad') is stated as the most correct and the view of the majority. This is the operative position for fatwa.
Also note the discussion on jahr and sirr.
5
Apply the ruling to a concrete scenario
Given a worshipper who forgetfully says 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad' without the family, according to the mufta bihi, sujud al-sahw is not required. If he adds 'wa 'ala aali Muhammad', then sahw is required.
This step ensures practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا قدم ركناً، أو أخره، أو كرره، أو غير واجباً، أو تركه ساهياً : كركوع قبل القراءة، وتأخير القيام إلى الثالثة بزيادة على التشهد. روي عن أبي حنيفة له أن من زاد على التشهد الأول حرفاً يجب عليه سجود السهو. اختلفوا فيه، فقيل: بوجوبه بمطلق الزيادة ولو بحرف، وقيل: بمقدار اللهم صل على محمد، وصححه الزيلعي في شرح الكنز، واختاره في البحر تبعاً لصاحب الخلاصة والخانية، والظاهر أنه لا يجب سجود السهو بقوله: اللهم صل على محمد، ونحوه، وإنما المعتبر مقدار ما يؤدي فيه ركناً، وركوعين، والجهر. وقيل: لا يجب ما لم يقل: وعلى آل محمد، قال الحلبي في شرح المنية الصغير: هو الأصح، وهو قول الأكثر. ثم اختلف في الجهر: فقيل: وجوب سجود السهو بالجهر في السرية على كل مصل إماماً كان أو منفرداً، وفي عكسه على الإمام دون المنفرد. فالحاصل أن الجهر في الجهريَّة لا يجب على المنفرد اتفاقاً، واختلف في وجوب السر عليه، وظاهر الرواية عدم الوجوب.
If one precedes a pillar, or delays it, or repeats it, or changes an obligatory act, or omits it forgetfully: such as bowing before recitation, and delaying the standing to the third [rak'ah] by an addition to the tashahhud. It is narrated from Abu Hanifa that whoever adds a single letter to the first tashahhud must perform sujud al-sahw. They differed: it was said that it is obligatory with any addition, even a letter; and it was said [obligatory] with the amount of 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad' and al-Zayla'i declared this correct in Sharh al-Kanz, and it was chosen in al-Bahr following the author of al-Khulasa and al-Khaniyya; and the apparent view is that sujud al-sahw is not obligatory for saying 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad' and similar, but what is considered is the amount in which one performs a pillar, two rak'ahs, and jahr. And it was said: it is not obligatory unless one says 'wa 'ala aali Muhammad'; al-Halabi said in Sharh al-Munyat al-Saghir: it is the most correct, and it is the view of the majority. Then they differed regarding jahr: it was said that the obligation of sujud al-sahw for jahr in a silent prayer applies to every worshipper, whether imam or individual, and for its opposite (silence in a loud prayer) it applies to the imam only, not the individual. The conclusion is that jahr in a loud prayer is not obligatory for the individual by agreement, and there is difference regarding the obligation of silence upon him, and the apparent transmission is non-obligation.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the conditions for sujud al-sahw due to an addition in the first tashahhud. The base text (matn) lists general actions that necessitate sahw, including an addition to tashahhud. The commentary expands by reporting a narration from Abu Hanifa that any single letter added necessitates sahw. However, the commentator notes a well-known ikhtilaf: some Hanafis (including Abu Yusuf and Muhammad according to some reports) hold that a mere letter does not obligate sahw; rather, the addition must be as much as the phrase 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad' (or more). This is the view preferred by al-Zayla'i and many later scholars. Others require the amount of performing a full pillar (like a rukn). The majority view, as stated by al-Halabi, is that sahw is not obligated unless one says 'wa 'ala aali Muhammad', i.e., a complete salutation on the Prophet and his family. The passage then briefly discusses jahr (loud recitation) and its opposite. The relied-upon position (mu'tamad) is that for jahr in a silent prayer, both the imam and the individual must perform sahw if they err; for silence in a loud prayer, only the imam bears the obligation. This is based on the principle that jahr and sirr are characteristics of the congregation, but the stronger view extends the obligation to the individual in the case of jahr in a silent prayer. The text notes that the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya) holds that there is no obligation for the individual regarding silence in a loud prayer.

Key Terms
سجود السهو (sujud al-sahw): Prostration of forgetfulness, performed to compensate for errors in prayer
التشهد الأول (al-tashahhud al-awwal): The first sitting and recitation in a prayer of two or more rak'ahs, before the final tashahhud
زيادة (ziyadah): An addition to the prescribed prayer, such as extra recitation or actions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct view (al-Halabi), what is the minimal addition to the first tashahhud that necessitates sujud al-sahw?
A) Any single letter
B) The phrase 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad'
C) The phrase 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad wa 'ala aali Muhammad'
D) The amount equal to performing a full pillar

Key Takeaway
The most correct Hanafi view is that sujud al-sahw is not required unless the extra recitation in the first tashahhud includes the full salawat on the Prophet and his family ('wa 'ala aali Muhammad').
