Salam with Intent to Cut Off After Forgetting a Wajib
Ruling on salam with intent to cut off after forgetting a wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving salam with the intention to exit the prayer when a wajib has been omitted, and identify the mufta bihi position within the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
If you intend to exit prayer with salam before performing wajib sajdat al-sahw, your intention is void and the prayer continues.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a person forgets a wajib in prayer, making sajdat al-sahw obligatory, but instead of performing it, he gives salam intending to exit the prayer. Does the salam end the prayer or not? The Hanafi school addresses this through the concept of tahrimah and the nature of salam as a muhallil. We will explore the ruling and the disagreement within the school between Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is giving salam with the intention to exit after forgetting a wajib. The ruling: intention is void, tahrimah remains, and the person must return to perform sajdat al-sahw.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is the inherent nature of salam as a muhallil. According to Imam Muhammad, the salam in this case is non-tahlili (not a valid exit). According to Sahibayn, it is tahlili on the basis of tawaqquf (suspension).
Both views derive from the same source, but differ on the effect of intention.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Why does intention matter? Because intending to make the salam a permanent exit changes the mashru' (legislated act) of the prayer. The intention to cut off alters the nature of the salam from an exit to an invalid act.
This is based on the principle that intentions can change the legal character of an action.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad holds that the salam is absolutely non-tahlili. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that it is tahlili on tawaqquf, meaning if intended as a permanent exit, it is invalid; if intended as conditional, it may be valid.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi (relied upon) position is that of Imam Muhammad: the salam does not exit the prayer, and the person must return to perform sajdat al-sahw.
The tarjih is based on caution and the authority of Imam Muhammad in this matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سَهَا وسلّم بنية القطع بطل نيته (۳) حتّى تكون تحريمتُه باقيةً كما مرّ. [١] قوله : سها وسلّم ؛ يعني سها عن واجب، فوجب عليه سجود السهو، وسلم بنية الخروج عن الصلاة فنيته باطلة، وتحريمته باقية، فعليه أن يعود إلى سجودِ السهو؛ لأنّ هذا السلام غير محلّل ع عند محمد الله ، ومتى قصد تحليله فقد قصد تغيّر المشروع، فلغت نيته. (٤) وعندهما: هو محلّل على سبيل التوقف، فمتى قصد أن يجعله محللا على الثبات، فقد قصد تغيير المشروع. كذا في «الكفاية»(۳).
He forgot and gave salam with the intention of cutting off; his intention is invalid. [Comment: He means he forgot an obligatory act, so prostration of forgetfulness became obligatory upon him, and he gave salam with the intention of exiting the prayer. So his intention is void, and his prohibition remains, so he must return to the prostration of forgetfulness. According to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), because this salam is not a permissible exit, and when he intended to make it permissible, he intended to change the legislated, so his intention is null. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is a permissible exit on the basis of suspension, so when he intended to make it a permanent permissible exit, he intended to change the legislated. Thus in al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This is a ruling on forgetfulness and giving salam with the intention to cut off the prayer. The passage first states the ruling: if someone forgets a wajib and then gives salam intending to leave the prayer, that intention is void. The prohibition (tahrimah) of the prayer remains intact. The commentator explains: by 'he forgot' it means he omitted a wajib, so sajdat al-sahw became necessary. He then gave salam intending to exit. According to Imam Muhammad, this salam is not a valid exit from the prayer; if he intended it as a valid exit, he intended to change the legislated, so his intention is nullified. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), the salam is a valid exit only on the basis of suspension (tawaqquf); if he intended to make it a permanent exit, he intended to change the legislated. Thus, the rule is that he must return and perform the sajdat al-sahw. The text references al-Kifayah as a source.

Key Terms
تحريمته (tahrimatuh): The state of prohibition that enters upon entering the prayer; the inviolability of the prayer.
محلل (muhallil): That which makes exiting the prayer permissible; a valid exit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The salam is valid and the person has exited the prayer.
C) The intention is void and the person must return to perform sajdat al-sahw.
D) The prayer is complete and no action is needed.

Key Takeaway
When a wajib is forgotten in prayer, giving salam with the intention to exit does not end the prayer; the individual must return to perform sajdat al-sahw.

Doubt in Rak'ahs: First-Time vs. Frequent – Hanafi Ruling
Ruling on doubt in rak'ahs: first time restart, frequent doubt act on predominant opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on doubt in the number of rak'ahs for a first-time doubter versus a frequent doubter, and apply the rules for seeking predominant opinion or taking the least.

Core Idea
Doubt in rak'ahs: first time restart; frequent doubt act on predominant opinion; if none, take the least and sit after each possible ending.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya addresses a common situation in salah: uncertainty about the number of rak'ahs prayed. The Hanafi school distinguishes between a first-time doubter and someone who frequently experiences doubt. The source reconciles apparently contradictory hadith to derive these rules, ensuring the prayer is valid while upholding the intent of the sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text begins with two distinct rulings: (1) if doubt occurs for the first time, restart the prayer (isti'naf); (2) if doubt is frequent, act on predominant opinion (ghalabat al-zann) and perform sajdat al-sahw at the end.
First time means from the age of puberty; frequent means it happens often, even if the exact count varies.
2
Analyze the sub-rule when predominant opinion is absent
If no predominant opinion arises, the ruling is to take the least number (e.g., three in a four-rak'ah prayer) and sit after that rak'ah because it could be the final sitting (fard). Then pray an additional rak'ah and sit again if needed.
The sitting after each potential last rak'ah is a precaution to avoid missing the obligatory final sitting.
3
Recognize the hadith evidence
The passage cites three types of hadith: (a) 'build on certainty' (take the least) – Muslim, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah; (b) 'seek the correct estimation' – Bukhari, Muslim; (c) Ibn Umar's practice of repeating. These appear contradictory.
Hanafi reconciles by assigning each hadith to a different scenario.
4
Understand the Hanafi reconciliation method
The Hanafi method: the hadith of 'building on certainty' applies to a first-time doubter (restart), the hadith of 'seeking correct estimation' applies to a frequent doubter, and Ibn Umar's repetition applies when after estimation nothing is clear.
This method is specific to Hanafi; other schools may not accept this reconciliation.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Given a scenario, determine whether the doubter is first-time or frequent, check if predominant opinion exists, and apply the corresponding rule. Then state the validity of the prayer.
If rules are followed, the prayer is valid (sahih); otherwise it may be invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شك أول مرة أنه كم صلى استأنف، وإن كثر أخذ ما غلب على ظنه، ويسجد في آخر صلاته. قوله: وإن لم يغلب أخذ الأقل، وقعد في كل موضع ظنه آخر صلاته؛ يعني إن شك أنه صلى ثلاث ركعات أو أربع ركعات، ولم يغلب على ظنه أحدهما أخذ بالأقل وهو الثلاث لكن يقعد ثم ثم يصلي ركعة أخرى، وإنما يقعد لأنه يمكن أن يكون آخر صلاته، والقعدة الأخيرة فرض. الباب روايات، فعند مسلم وأبي داود وابن ماجة وغيرهم مرفوعاً: إذا شك أحدكم في صلاته فلم يدر أثلاثاً صلى أم أربعاً، فليلق الشك ويبن على اليقين؛ أي المتيقن وهو الأقل، وعند الشيخين وغيرهما مرفوعاً: إذا شك أحدكم فليتحر الصواب فليتم عليه. وروى ابن أبي شيبة عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنه: أما أنا فإذا لم أدر كم صليت فإني أعيد، ومثله روى عن جمع من التابعين، فجمع بينها أصحابنا بأن حملوا الأخير على ما إذا عرض له الشك أول مرة، والثاني على ما إذا كثر ذلك، والأول على ما لم يتبين شيء بعد التحري، وهذه الطريقة من إلغاء واحد من الأخبار بالكلية. قوله: لأنه يمكن؛ يعني يمكن أن تكون هذه الركعة في الواقع آخر ركعاته، فلو لم يقعد يلزم ترك الركن.
If he doubts for the first time how many he has prayed, he restarts; if it becomes frequent, he acts on his predominant opinion and prostrates at the end of his prayer. His statement: 'If it does not become predominant, he takes the least and sits in every place he thinks is the end of his prayer' means: if he doubts whether he prayed three or four rak'ahs and neither is predominant, he takes the least, which is three, but he sits there, then prays another rak'ah, and he sits because it could be the end of his prayer, and the final sitting is obligatory. The chapter contains narrations: according to Muslim, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, and others, marfu': 'When one of you doubts in his prayer and does not know whether he prayed three or four, let him throw away the doubt and build on certainty' i.e., the certain which is the least. According to the two shaykhs and others, marfu': 'When one of you doubts, let him seek the correct (estimation) and complete upon it.' And Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated from Ibn Umar radiyallahu anhuma: 'As for me, when I do not know how many I have prayed, I repeat it.' And similarly from a group of tabi'un. So our companions reconciled them by interpreting the last as when doubt occurs for the first time, the second as when it is frequent, and the first as when nothing is clarified after estimation. And this method entirely cancels one of the narrations.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage deals with doubt in prayer regarding the number of rak'ahs. The initial ruling: if doubt occurs for the first time (from the age of puberty), the person must restart the prayer from the beginning. But if doubt becomes frequent (i.e., it happens often), then one acts on his predominant opinion (ghalabat al-zann) and prostrates for forgetfulness at the end. If even after estimation no predominant opinion arises, he takes the least number he is certain of (e.g., three in a four-rak'ah prayer), but he must sit after that rak'ah because it could be the final sitting, which is fard. Then he prays another rak'ah and another sitting, etc. The passage then reconciles conflicting hadith: the hadith of 'building on certainty' is applied to first-time doubter; the hadith of 'seek the correct estimation' is for frequent doubt; and the narration of Ibn Umar 'I repeat' is for when after estimation nothing is clear. This method fails to fully reconcile all narrations but is the Hanafi method.

Key Terms
التحري (al-taharrī): Acting on predominant opinion after careful consideration.
الاستئناف (al-isti'naf): Restarting the prayer from the beginning.
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Predominant opinion, strong inclination towards one possibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He ignores the doubt and continues.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between first-time and frequent doubt in rak'ahs: first-time requires restart, frequent allows acting on predominant opinion, and if none, take the least with precautionary sittings.

Ruling on Doubt in Rak'ahs When No Predominant Assumption
Explains the Hanafi ruling for doubt in rak'ahs with reconciliation of hadiths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for doubt in rak'ahs when neither number preponderates, and explain how the Hanafis reconcile conflicting hadiths.

Core Idea
When doubt in rak'ahs has no preponderance, take the lesser number and sit—because the final sitting is an integral pillar.

When a worshipper loses count during prayer and cannot determine whether he prayed three or four rak'ahs, the Hanafi school provides a clear method based on certainty. This lesson unpacks that ruling, the proofs from hadith, and how the Hanafi jurists reconciled apparently contradictory narrations without discarding any.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage addresses doubt in the number of rak'ahs (e.g., three or four) where neither side preponderates in the mind. This is the specific case for which the ruling is given.
The doubt must be genuine; if one side is stronger, the ruling differs (act on the stronger assumption).
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The ruling: take the lesser number (the certain one), sit (perform tashahhud) as if it were the final rak'ah, then pray an additional rak'ah to complete the prayer.
The sitting is obligatory because the final sitting is a rukn; omitting it would invalidate the prayer.
3
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'If one doubts in his prayer, let him cast aside doubt and build upon certainty' (Muslim, Abu Dawud, etc.). The Hanafis interpret 'certainty' as the lesser number.
This hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but sufficient for establishing the ruling.
4
Identify the ikhtilaf in narrations
Other hadiths command seeking the correct opinion (taharrī) or repeating the prayer (from Ibn 'Umar). These appear to contradict the first hadith.
The Hanafis do not reject any narration but reconcile them.
5
Explain the Hanafi reconciliation
First-time doubt → build on certainty (take lesser). Frequent doubt → seek the correct opinion (taharrī). If nothing becomes clear after seeking → take the lesser and sit (return to the first hadith). This method applies each hadith to its proper context.
The reconciliation avoids discarding any authentic report and shows the depth of Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يغلب أخذ الأقل، وقعد في كل موضع ظنّه آخر صلاته لأنه إذا كثر كان في الاستئناف حرج ، وإن لم يغلب أخذ الأقل، وقعد في كل موضع ظنه آخر صلاته : يعني إن شك أنه صلى ثلاث ركعات، أو أربع ركعات، ولم يغلب على ظنه أحدهما أخذ بالأقل، وهو الثلاث لكن يقعد ثمة، ثم يصلي ركعة أخرى، وإنما يقعد؛ لأنه يمكن أن يكون آخر صلاته، والقعدة الأخيرة فرض. وفي الباب روايات، فعند مسلم وأبي داود وابن ماجة وغيرهم مرفوعاً: «إذا شك أحدكم في صلاته فلم يدر أثلاثاً صلّى أم أربعاً، فليلق الشك وليبن على اليقين»؛ أي المتيقن وهو الأقل، وعند الشيخين وغيرهما مرفوعاً: «إذا شك أحدكم فليتحرَّ الصواب، فليتم عليه». وروى ابن أبي شيبة عن ابن عمر الله : «أما أنا فإذا لم أدر كم صليت، فإنّي أعيد»، ومثله رواه عن جمع من التابعين، فجمع بينها أصحابنا بأن حملوا الأول على ما إذا عرض له الشك أول مرة، والثاني على ما إذا كثر ذلك، والأول على ما لم يتبين شيء بعد التحري، وهذه طريقة من إلغاء واحد من الأخبار بالكلية. قوله : لأنه يمكن؛ يعني يمكن أن تكون هذه الركعة في الواقع آخر ركعاته، فلو لم يقعد يلزم ترك الركن. وقوله : ظنه آخر صلاته ليس المراد بالظنِّ رجحان أحد الطرفين، بل المراد الوهم؛ لأنَّ المفروض أنه لم يغلب أحد الطرفين على الآخر. والله أعلم. قوله : وقوله ظنه.... الخ؛ دفع دخل مقدر، تقرير الدخل أن «قوله» في صورة عدم غلبة شيء على الظنّ، قَعَدَ في كلّ موضع ظنّه آخر صلاته غير صحيح؛ لأنَّ الظنَّ عبارة عن رجحان أحد الطرفين، وهو مفقود في هذه الصورة، إذ المفروض أنه لم يغلب على ظنّه شيء، وإلا فكان عليه أن يأخذ بما ظنّه لا بالأقل. وتحرير الدفع : أنّ الظَّنَّ قد يطلق على الوهم أيضاً، وهو المراد هاهنا لا رجحان أحد الطرفين، وقد يقال : الوهم عبارة عن الطرف المرجوح، وهو لا يوجد إلا عند وجود الظنّ، وإذا ليس هاهنا ظنّ، فلا وهم، ويجاب عنه بأن المراد بالوهم مجرد التخييل.
And if [one side] does not predominate, he takes the lesser, and sits in every place where he thinks it is the end of his prayer. Because if it becomes frequent, there is hardship in restarting. And if [one side] does not predominate, he takes the lesser, and sits in every place where he thinks it is the end of his prayer. Meaning: If he doubts whether he prayed three rak'ahs or four rak'ahs, and neither preponderates in his mind, he takes the lesser, which is three, but he sits there, then prays another rak'ah. He sits because it could be the end of his prayer, and the final sitting is obligatory. In this chapter there are narrations: According to Muslim, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah and others, raised [to the Prophet]: "If one of you doubts in his prayer and does not know whether he prayed three or four, let him cast aside doubt and build upon certainty" – that is, the certain, which is the lesser. And according to al-Bukhari and Muslim and others, raised: "If one of you doubts, let him seek the correct [opinion] and complete based on it." And Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated from Ibn 'Umar: "As for me, if I do not know how much I have prayed, I repeat it," and similar was narrated from a group of the Tabi'in. Our companions reconciled them by carrying the first [narration] on the case where doubt occurs for the first time, the second on when it becomes frequent, and the first [i.e., the one about repeating] on the case where nothing becomes clear after seeking the correct opinion. And this method avoids completely discarding any of the reports. His statement: "because it is possible" means it is possible that this rak'ah is in reality the last of his rak'ahs, so if he does not sit, he would omit an integral part. And his statement: "he thinks it is the end of his prayer" does not intend by 'thinking' the preponderance of one side, but rather intends 'illusion', because the assumption is that neither side preponderates over the other. And Allah knows best. His statement: "And his statement: 'he thinks...' etc." is a defence against an anticipated objection. The formulation of the objection is that his statement 'sits in every place where he thinks it is the end of his prayer' in the case of non-preponderance of anything is incorrect, because 'thinking' (zann) is the preponderance of one side, and that is absent in this case, since it is assumed that nothing preponderates in his mind; otherwise he would be required to act on what he thinks, not on the lesser. The resolution: that 'thinking' (zann) may also be applied to 'illusion' (wahm), and that is intended here, not preponderance of one side. It may be said: 'illusion' is the weaker side, and it only exists when there is 'thinking', and since there is no 'thinking' here, there is no illusion. The response is that what is meant by 'illusion' is mere imagination.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school on how to handle doubt in the number of rak'ahs during prayer when neither number is more likely. The author first states the ruling: take the lesser number (the one you are certain of), sit after that rak'ah (because it might be the final salah), then pray an additional rak'ah to complete the prayer. The reasoning is that if doubt becomes frequent, restarting would cause hardship. The author then cites several hadiths that seem contradictory: some command building on certainty (the lesser), some command seeking the correct opinion, and some suggest repeating the prayer. The Hanafis reconcile these by categorizing: for first-time doubt, one should build on certainty; for frequent doubt, one should act on predominant assumption; and for doubt where neither side preponderates even after seeking, one takes the lesser and sits. This method avoids discarding any of the narrations. The passage ends with a terminological clarification: the word 'zann' (ظن) in the phrase 'he thinks it is the end of his prayer' does not mean strong probability but rather mere illusion or imagination, because the scenario assumes no preponderance. This addresses a potential objection that 'zann' requires preponderance. The Hanafis explain that 'zann' is used here in a broader sense to include weak conjecture.

Key Terms
الظن (al-zann): In this passage, it is used broadly to include mere illusion or imagination, not strong probability.
الوهم (al-wahm): The weaker side in a doubt, or mere imagination without preponderance.
التحري (al-taharrī): Seeking the correct opinion or trying to determine what is more likely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, what should a person do if he doubts whether he has prayed three or four rak'ahs and neither number seems more likely?
A) Repeat the entire prayer.
B) Take the lesser number (three), sit, then pray one more rak'ah.
C) Act on whatever comes to mind first.
D) Ignore the doubt and continue.

Key Takeaway
When doubt in prayer has no preponderant side, the Hanafi ruling is to take the lesser number, sit, then complete the prayer, based on the hadith of building on certainty.

Meaning of Zann in Sujud al-Sahw
Clarifies that 'zann' means wahm in sujud al-sahw.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school interprets 'zann' as wahm in sujud al-sahw and apply this refined definition to correctly resolve doubts during prayer.

Core Idea
In sujud al-sahw, 'zann' means a faint impression, not a predominant belief.

The Hanafi rule for doubt in prayer states that if a worshipper has no predominant assumption, he should sit after every two rak'ats, thinking it is his last. But 'zann' usually means predominant assumption. How can this rule make sense? This lesson unpacks the commentary that saves the consistency of the school's ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Recognize that the standard meaning of 'zann' is a predominant assumption (ghalabat al-zann). The rule is invoked when there is no such predominance, so the wording seems contradictory. State the objection clearly.
2
Understand the commentator's solution
The commentator clarifies that 'zann' here is used to mean 'wahm' (a mere illusion or faint impression). This allows the rule to apply even when no side is stronger. Grasp this terminological shift.
3
Apply the refined definition
Now apply: when a worshipper has a faint, non-dominant thought that he may be at the end of prayer, he should sit after every two rak'ats and perform sujud al-sahw. This is the Hanafi position.
This prevents the prayer from being invalidated due to uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : ظنه آخر صلاته ليس المراد بالظَّنِّ رجحان أحد الطرفين، بل المراد الوهم ; لأنَّ المفروض أنه لم يغلب أحد الطرفين على الآخر. والله أعلم. [١] قوله : وقوله ظنه.... الخ ; دفع دخل مقدر ، تقرير الدخل أن «قوله» في صورة عدم غلبة شيء على الظنّ، قَعَدَ في كلّ موضع ظنّه آخر صلاته غير صحيح; لأنَّ الظنَّ عبارة عن رجحان أحد الطرفين، وهو مفقود في هذه الصورة، إذ المفروض أنه لم يغلب على ظنّه شيء، وإلا فكان عليه أن يأخذ بما ظنّه لا بالأقل. وتحرير الدفع : أنّ الظَّنَّ قد يطلق على الوهم أيضاً، وهو المراد هاهنا لا رجحان أحد الطرفين، وقد يقال : الوهم عبارة عن الطرف المرجوح، وهو لا يوجد إلا عند وجود الظنّ، وإذا ليس هاهنا ظنّ ، فلا وهم، ويجاب عنه بأن المراد بالوهم مجرد التخييل.
And his statement: 'he thought it was his last prayer' – the intended meaning of 'zann' is not the predominance of one of the two possibilities, but rather the intended meaning is wahm (illusion), because the assumption is that neither possibility predominated over the other. And Allah knows best. [1] The author's statement: 'and his statement: he thought... etc.' – it is a refutation of an anticipated objection. The formulation of the objection is that his statement, in the scenario where nothing predominates over the assumption, that he sits in every place where he thinks it is his last prayer is not correct, because zann is an expression for the predominance of one of the two possibilities, and it is absent in this scenario, for it is assumed that nothing predominated over his assumption. Otherwise, he would have to act on what he thinks, not on the lesser. And the formulation of the refutation is that zann may also be applied to wahm, and that is what is intended here, not the predominance of one of the two possibilities. And it may be said: wahm is an expression for the weaker possibility, and it does not exist except when zann exists. And if there is no zann here, then there is no wahm. And it is answered that what is meant by wahm is mere illusion.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from a commentary (sharh) or gloss on the Hidayah. It addresses a potential objection concerning the use of the word 'zann' in the rule for sujud al-sahw. The rule states that if a person has no predominant assumption about his remaining prayers, he should act on the lesser number and sit after every two rak'ats, treating each as his last. The objection is that 'zann' means predominant assumption, but in this scenario there is no such predominance, so the phrasing is contradictory. The commentator refutes this by explaining that 'zann' here is used linguistically to mean 'wahm' (illusion or mere impression), not predominant assumption. Thus, the rule is sound. This distinction is important for correct application of sujud al-sahw rules in the Hanafi school. The key point is that even a faint, non-dominant thought can trigger the obligation to sit and treat the prayer as unfinished.

Key Terms
الظن (az-zann): In this text, zann is used to mean wahm (illusion/delusion), not the predominant assumption or preponderance of opinion.
الوهم (al-wahm): A weaker or illusory thought that does not reach the level of predominant assumption. In this context, it is the intended meaning of 'zann'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Illusion or delusion (wahm)
D) Doubt (shakk)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets 'zann' in the sujud al-sahw rule as wahm (faint impression), not predominant assumption, to consistently handle cases of non-dominating doubt.

Prayer of the Sick: Graded Concessions and Definition of Inability
Ruling on prayer of the sick with hadith proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the graded concessions for a sick person's prayer and apply the Hanafi definition of inability (ta'adhdhur) to determine whether standing is excused.

Core Idea
Prayer is never dropped; its mode adapts to genuine inability.

This passage from the sharh of al-Wiqayah opens the chapter on the prayer of the sick. It presents the core ruling: if standing is difficult, one sits; if even bowing and prostration are difficult, one gestures with the head. But what counts as 'difficult'? The Hanafi school defines it not only as actual impossibility but also as harm that would result from standing. This lesson unpacks that ruling, its evidence, and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the graded concessions
The matn presents a sequence: standing → sitting with ruku and sujud → sitting with gestures (ima'). Note each level and what triggers the move to the next.
The hadith adds a fourth level: lying on one's side gesturing.
2
Extract the definition of ta'adhdhur
The sharh explains that 'difficult' includes both actual inability (would fall) and legal inability (harm would befall). The relied-upon view from al-Nihayah: harm would result if standing.
This is broader than mere discomfort or tiredness.
3
Locate the primary evidence
The hadith 'Pray standing; if not, then sitting; if not, then on your side gesturing' is the proof. It is narrated by Bukhari and others.
The imperative 'صل' indicates obligation, and the conditionals show the concession.
4
Distinguish obligatory vs supererogatory
The commentary clarifies that the concession applies only to obligatory prayers. For supererogatory prayer, sitting is permissible even without inability, based on the hadith of Imran ibn Husain.
This is a specific Hanafi view.
5
Apply to partial inability
If one cannot stand fully but can stand partially, one stands to the extent possible and sits for the remainder.
The ruling follows ability as much as possible.

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the chapter on the prayer of the sick (Bab Salat al-Mareed) from the sharh. It states the basic ruling: if standing is difficult due to illness, one prays sitting with bowing and prostration; if even bowing and prostration are difficult, one gestures with the head, making prostration lower than bowing. The commentary then expands on each phrase: 'if difficult' applies to obligatory prayers; for supererogatory, sitting is allowed even without difficulty, based on hadith. 'Difficult' includes both actual inability (e.g., would fall) and legal inability (harm would result). 'Standing' means all of it; if partial inability, one stands as much as possible. The basis for the ruling is the hadith from Bukhari and others: 'Pray standing; if you cannot, then sitting; if you cannot, then on your side, gesturing.' A second hadith specifies that prostration gesture should be lower than bowing. The commentary also cites the hadith of Imran ibn Husain about sitting prayer having half the reward, which shows its permissibility. The Hanafi school holds that these concessions are based on genuine inability, and the obligation to pray remains even in severe illness.

Key Terms
التعذر (at-ta'adhdhur): Inability to perform an act due to illness; includes both actual impossibility and legal harm.
الإيماء (al-ima'): Gesturing with a part of the body, usually the head, to indicate bowing and prostration when unable to perform actual movements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Point with his finger

Key Takeaway
The prayer of the sick is valid when adapted to genuine inability, defined as harm or fall if attempted to stand.

Ruling on the Prayer of the Sick Person
Ruling on prayer when unable to stand, bow, or prostrate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sequence of alternative postures for the sick person's obligatory prayer and apply the Hanafi definition of 'impossibility' (ta'adhur) to determine when standing is excused.

Core Idea
Pray according to ability: standing → sitting → gesturing, impossibility defined by harm.

This passage from the chapter on prayer of the sick addresses a fundamental fiqh principle: obligations are scaled to capacity. The author outlines a clear sequence of postures when a worshipper cannot stand, bow, or prostrate due to illness. We will examine the textual evidence (dalil) and the Hanafi definition of 'impossibility' (ta'adhur), which includes harm as a valid excuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling and its sequence
The text states: if standing is impossible → pray sitting with normal ruku and sujud; if ruku and sujud are impossible → gesture with the head while sitting, making the gesture for sujud lower; if sitting is impossible → lie on the back and gesture. This is the fixed sequence for the obligatory prayer.
For supererogatory prayers, sitting is allowed even if able to stand.
2
Clarify the definition of 'ta'adhur' (impossibility)
The commentary explains that 'ta'adhur' includes both real impossibility (e.g. falling if one tries to stand) and legal impossibility (harm). The relied-upon definition for fatwa is that standing causes harm.
Harm can be physical pain or exacerbation of illness.
3
Examine the textual evidence (dalil)
The primary hadith is: 'Pray standing; if you cannot, then sitting; if you cannot, then on your side, gesturing.' This is from Bukhari and the Sunan. Another hadith from al-Bazzar specifies making prostration gesture lower than bowing. The hadith of 'Imran adds the lying-on-back position.
The imperative 'صل' indicates obligation (wujub) of standing when able.
4
Address the secondary issue: raising something for prostration
The text rules that it is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) to raise an object to the forehead for prostration. If done, the prayer still suffices as long as the gesture is lower. Placing a pillow on the ground and prostrating on it is permissible.
The prohibition is based on a hadith; the act resembles innovation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صلاة المريض
إن تعذر القيام لمرض حدث قبل الصلاة أو فيها صلى قاعداً يركع ويسجد، وإن تعذرا أوما برأسه قاعداً، وجعل سجوده أخفض من ركوعه.
(إن تعذر القيام لمرض حدث قبل الصلاة أو فيها صلى قاعداً يركع ويسجد. وإن تعذرا) أي الركوع والسجود، (أوما برأسه قاعداً، وجعل سجوده أخفض من ركوعه).
قوله: باب صلاة المريض; مناسبة بما سبق من أن كلاً من سجدة السهو وصلاة المريض بعارض سماوي، فناسب ذكرهما متصلاً، ولما كان الأول أكثر وقوعاً قدمه. (٢) قوله: إن تعذر; أي في المكتوبة، وأما التطوع فجائز قاعداً أيضاً مع قدرته على القيام لما ثبت ذلك في الأحاديث المروية الصحيحة في الصحاح السنة وغيرها. [٣] قوله: إن تعذر; المراد به أعم من التعذر الحقيقي، بأن يكون بحيث لو قام سقط من التعذر الحكمي، وحده على المفتى به كما في «النهاية»: أن يلحقه ضرر بالقيام. [٤] قوله: القيام; أي كله، فإن تعذر بعضه قام بقدر عدم التعذر، وقعد بعد. [٦] قوله: وجعل; لحديث: «إن استطعت وإلا فأومئ إيماء، واجعل سجودك أخفض من الركوع»، أخرجه البزار، وغيره.
ولا يرفع إليه شيئاً للسجود، وإن تعذر القعود أوما مستلقياً ورجلاه إلى القبلة.
قوله: ولا يرفع، بصيغة المجهول، وهو مكروه تحريماً; لثبوت النهي عنه، فإن فعل ذلك وسجوده أخفض أجزأ ذلك، ومعنى الرفع: أن يحمل شيء إلى وجهه; ليسجد عليه، وإن كانت الوسادة موضوعة على الأرض وسجد عليها جائز، كذا في «الذخيرة». [٢] قوله: وإن تعذر القعود; أي مطلقاً بأن لا يقدر عليه لا بنفسه ولا مستنداً إلى جدار، أو خادم، فإن قدر عليه لزمه ذلك، كما إذا قدر على القيام متكئاً على عصاً، أو خادم، فإن الحلواني قال: الصحيح أنه يلزم القيام. كذا في «الغنية». [٣] قوله: أومأ مستلقياً; أي على ظهره جاعلاً وسادة تحت كتفيه ماداً رجليه; ليتمكن من الإيماء، وإلا فحقيقة الاستلقاء تمنع الصحيح من الإيماء، فكيف المريض. كذا في «الغنية». والأصل فيه حديث عمران: أنه كانت به بواسير، فسأل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن الصلاة، فقال: «صل قائماً، فإن لم تستطع فعلى جنب»، أخرجه مسلم وأصحاب السنن، زاد النسائي: «فإن لم تستطع فمستلقياً لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها».
Chapter on the Prayer of the Sick
If standing becomes impossible due to an illness that occurs before or during the prayer, he prays sitting, bowing and prostrating. If both (bowing and prostration) become impossible, he gestures with his head while sitting, and makes his prostration lower than his bowing.
(If standing becomes impossible due to an illness that occurs before or during the prayer, he prays sitting, bowing and prostrating. And if both [i.e., bowing and prostration] become impossible, he gestures with his head while sitting, and makes his prostration lower than his bowing.)
His saying: Chapter on the Prayer of the Sick: The connection to what preceded is that both the prostration of forgetfulness and the prayer of the sick are due to a heavenly accident, so it is appropriate to mention them consecutively. And since the first is more frequent, it was placed before. (2) His saying: If it becomes impossible: Meaning in the obligatory prayer. As for supererogatory prayer, it is permissible sitting even if able to stand, based on what is established in authentic hadiths in the six books and others. [3] His saying: If it becomes impossible: The intended meaning is more general than real impossibility, such that if he were to stand he would fall; this is legal impossibility. Its definition according to the fatwa, as in al-Nihaya: that harm affects him from standing. [4] His saying: Standing: i.e., all of it. So if part of it is impossible, he stands to the extent of non-impossibility, and sits afterwards. [0] His saying: He prays sitting: This is a circumstantial adverb, and likewise what follows are two overlapping or synonymous circumstantial adverbs. This is due to the hadith: 'Pray standing, and if you cannot, then sitting; if you cannot, then on your side, gesturing with gestures.' Narrated by Bukhari and the authors of the Sunan. [6] His saying: And he makes: Due to the hadith: 'If you can, otherwise gesture with gestures, and make your prostration lower than your bowing.' Narrated by al-Bazzar and others.
And nothing is raised to him for prostration. If sitting becomes impossible, he gestures while lying on his back with his feet towards the qibla.
His saying: And nothing is raised: In the passive voice. It is prohibitively disliked due to the prohibition being established. If he does that and his prostration is lower, it suffices. The meaning of raising is that something is carried to his face for him to prostrate on. If the pillow is placed on the ground and he prostrates on it, it is permissible. As in al-Dhakhira. [2] His saying: And if sitting becomes impossible: i.e., absolutely, such that he cannot do it by himself or leaning against a wall or a servant. If he is able, it is obligatory for him. Similarly, if he is able to stand leaning on a stick or a servant, al-Halwani said: The correct view is that standing is obligatory. As in al-Ghunya. [3] His saying: He gestures while lying on his back: i.e., on his back, placing a pillow under his shoulders, stretching his legs to be able to gesture. Otherwise, the reality of lying on the back prevents the healthy person from gesturing, so how about the sick? As in al-Ghunya. The basis for it is the hadith of 'Imran: He had hemorrhoids, so he asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) about prayer, and he said: 'Pray standing; if you cannot, then on your side.' Narrated by Muslim and the authors of the Sunan. Al-Nasa'i added: 'If you cannot, then lying on your back; Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.'
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on the prayer of the sick. The author begins by stating the ruling: if a person is unable to stand due to an illness that occurs before or during the prayer, he prays sitting, performing bowing and prostration normally. If he is also unable to bow and prostrate, then he gestures with his head while sitting, making the gesture for prostration lower than that for bowing. The commentary then explains that this applies to obligatory prayers; for supererogatory prayers, sitting is permissible even if able to stand, based on authentic hadiths. The term 'ta'adhur' (impossibility) is clarified: it includes both real impossibility (e.g., if standing would cause falling) and legal impossibility (if standing would cause harm). The definition for fatwa is that standing causes harm. If only part of standing is possible, one stands as much as possible and sits afterwards. The hadith 'Pray standing...' from Bukhari and others is cited as evidence. Another hadith from al-Bazzar supports making prostration lower. Then the text addresses the situation when sitting is also impossible: the person gestures while lying on his back with feet towards the qibla. There is a prohibition against raising something to the forehead for prostration; this is prohibitively disliked. However, if one does it and the prostration is lower, it still suffices. Raising means carrying an object to the face to prostrate on, which is different from having a pillow on the ground and prostrating on it (that is permissible). The commentary further explains that if one cannot sit at all, even with support, then he gestures lying on his back. The hadith of 'Imran is presented as the basis, with the addition from al-Nasa'i that lying on the back is permissible when unable to sit.

Key Terms
تعذر (ta'adhara): Inability that makes standing impossible, either genuinely or legally due to harm.
إيماء (īmā'): Gesturing with the head for bowing and prostration when unable to perform them physically.
مستلقياً (mustalqiyan): Lying on the back with a pillow under the shoulders and legs stretched towards the qibla, able to gesture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a sick person who cannot stand or sit, but can gesture while lying on his back?
A) He is exempt from prayer until he recovers.
B) He prays lying on his back with gestures, feet towards qibla.
C) He prays sitting on a chair if available.
D) He must repeat the prayer later when able.

Key Takeaway
A sick person prays according to ability: standing if possible without harm, otherwise sitting, then gesturing, then lying on back, each step only when the previous is truly impossible.

The Physical Postures for Ima (Pointing) in a Sick Person's Prayer
Commentary on sick person's pointing postures and limits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the positions for ima when a sick person cannot pray normally, and apply the evidence from the hadith of Imran bin Husayn.

Core Idea
Ima is only with the head; the sick person uses the easiest possible posture while maintaining qibla orientation.

When illness prevents normal prayer movements, Islamic law provides a clear sequence of concessions. The Prophet (peace be upon him) taught Imran bin Husayn a graded approach: standing, sitting, lying on the side, then on the back. This lesson explains the physical postures for ima and the prohibition of lifting objects for prostration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Ima (pointing with the head) is a substitute for ruku and sujud when physically unable to perform them. The ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) and when needed, it becomes obligatory to perform the prayer in this manner.
The basis is the hadith 'Pray standing, if you cannot, then on your side...'
2
Determine the posture when sitting is impossible
If the sick person cannot sit at all (even with support), he should lie on his back (mustalqi) with his feet toward the qibla and gesture with his head. The commentator explains that a pillow is placed under the shoulders to allow gesturing.
The posture must facilitate facing qibla as much as possible.
3
Consider the alternative posture
Lying on the side (mudtaji') – on the right or left side with face toward qibla – is also permissible. However, lying on the back (mustalqi) is better because it allows more direct facing of the qibla.
The right side is preferred over the left based on transmitted reports (athar).
4
Trace the evidence
The primary evidence is the hadith of Imran bin Husayn: the Prophet ﷺ said, 'Pray standing; if you cannot, then sitting; if you cannot, then on your side; if you cannot, then on your back – Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.' This shows the sequential concessions.
The version with 'on your back' is added in some narrations (al-Nasa'i).
5
Apply the rule when even head gesture is impossible
If even ima with the head is impossible, the prayer is delayed until the person is able to perform it in some valid form. This is based on the principle that Allah does not burden beyond capacity.
This delay is not abandonment but postponement until capacity returns.

Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of Salat al-Marid (Prayer of the Sick) from Al-Wiqaya. It focuses on the rulings of ima (pointing/gesturing) for a sick person who cannot perform the normal postures. The commentator, al-Shurunbulali, expands on the base text (matn) and provides proofs from hadith and rational arguments.

The first ruling states that it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) to lift an object to the face to prostrate on it, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade this. If one does it, the prayer still suffices as long as his prostration is lower than his ruku. However, if the object (e.g., a pillow) is placed on the ground and he prostrates on it, it is permissible; the prohibition is only for raising it.

The second ruling deals with the position for ima when sitting is impossible. The sick person lies on his back (mustalqi) with feet toward the qibla and gestures with his head. The commentary clarifies that if he can sit with support, he must; otherwise, he uses this position. The hadith of Imran bin Husayn is cited as the primary evidence, which shows progression: standing > sitting > on side > on back.

The third part discusses lying on the side (mudtaji'). The matn says the first (lying on back) is better. The commentator explains this is because on the back, the person faces the qibla more directly, whereas on the side he is turned away.

Then the text addresses the case where even head gesture (ima) is impossible. In that case, the prayer is delayed until the person can perform it in some form. The commentator emphasizes that Allah does not burden beyond capacity.

A key dispute is mentioned: contrary to Zufar, the Hanafi school holds that one cannot substitute head gestures with eye, eyebrow, or heart gestures. The reason is that the Sunnah only transmitted head gestures, and analogical substitution (nusb al-abdal bil ra'y) is not allowed in this matter.

Finally, the passage addresses the scenario where a person can stand but cannot bow or prostrate. According to Abu Hanifa (and the Hanafi school), he should sit and gesture with his head, and this is better than gesturing while standing. This is contrary to Zufar and the three imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali), who require standing and gesturing because standing is a pillar. The Hanafi response is that standing is a means (wasila) to prostration, and prostration is the actual pillar; hence, when the end (sujud) cannot be achieved, the means (standing) is not required.

Key Terms
الإيماء (al-ima'): Pointing or gesturing with the head to indicate bowing and prostration when unable to perform them physically.
المستلقي (al-mustalqi): Lying on the back, with legs extended, head slightly raised, facing qibla, used for ima when cannot sit.
المضطجع (al-mudtaji'): Lying on the side (right or left) with face toward qibla, used for ima.
مكروه تحريما (makruh tahrimi): A disliked act that is closer to haram; based on a strong (zanni) prohibition. Avoidance is required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which posture for ima is considered better according to the Hanafi school when sitting is impossible?
A) Lying on the right side (mudtaji')
B) Lying on the back (mustalqi)
C) Lying on the left side
D) Standing and gesturing

Key Takeaway
Ima is performed by head gestures only; the sick person uses the easiest possible posture while maximizing qibla orientation, and if even head gesture is impossible, the prayer is delayed.

The Sick Person's Prayer: When to Sit and Gesture
States rulings for prayer when unable to perform full movements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a sick person who cannot bow or prostrate and apply the principle of wasilah to determine whether to sit or stand.

Core Idea
When prostration is impossible, standing is dropped — a means falls when the goal is unreachable.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary addresses a common question: How should a sick person pray when they cannot perform the full movements? The commentary explains the preferred positions and gestures, and provides proofs for why sitting is better than standing when one cannot bow or prostrate. It also distinguishes the Hanafi view from that of other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the obligatory prayer performed by a sick person who is unable to perform ruku' and sujud but can stand. The ruling is that sitting and gesturing with the head is permissible and better (afdal).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof includes: the Quranic principle 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (Quran 2:286) and the qiyas that standing is a means (wasila) to prostration, which is the goal (maqsud).
Also referenced: the hadith that only head gesture is established, preventing substitution with eyes.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Quranic verse shows that if bowing and prostrating are impossible, the obligation is reduced. The qiyas shows that standing is only a means; when the goal (prostration) is impossible, the means drops, analogous to wudu being dropped when prayer is impossible.
Prostration is valid without standing, as in sajda al-tilawa.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Zufar (Hanafi) and the three imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) require standing and gesturing because standing is a pillar (rukn) that cannot be abandoned when possible.
Intra-Hanafi: Zufar allows eye gesture; Abu Hanifa disallows it.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position (sitting and gesturing) is mufta bihi because the proof from wasilah is stronger and provides ease for the sick.
The three imams' position is more difficult; Hanafis prioritize the goal over the means.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو مضطجعا ووجهه إليها والأول أولى وإن تعذر الإيماء أخرت ولا يومئ بعينيه خلافا لزفر له وحاجبيه وقلبه وإن تعذر الركوع والسجود لا القيام قعد وأومأ وهو أفضل من الإيماء قائما. قوله: أو مضطجعا أي على جنبه الأيمن أو الأيسر والأول أولى. كذا في إمداد الفتاح للشرنبلالي. قوله: أولى لأن المستلقي يكون توجهه إلى القبلة أكثر والمضطجع يكون منحرفا عنها. قوله: أخرت أي تركت الصلاة وأخرت عن وقتها إلى أن يقدر على صورة من صور أدائها لأن الله لا يكلف نفسا إلا وسعها. قوله: ولا يومى بعينيه أي لا يجوز له عند تعذر الإيماء بالرأس أن يشير بالعينين أو بالحاجبين أو بالقلب لأن الثابت في الأحاديث إنما هو الإيماء بالرأس ونصب الأبدال بالرأي ممتنع. قوله: قعد قال في المنية والغنية: إن قدر المريض على القيام دون الركوع والسجود لم يلزمه القيام عندنا بل يجوز أن يؤدي قاعدا وهو أفضل خلافا لزفر والأئمة الثلاثة فإن عندهم يلزم أن يومي قائما لأن القيام ركن فلا يتركه مع القدرة عليه. ولنا: أن القيام وسيلة إلى السجود والسجود أصل بدليل أن السجود شرعا عبادة بدون القيام كما في سجدة التلاوة والقيام لم يشرع عبادة وحده ولو سجد لغير الله يكفر ولا كذلك القيام فإذا عجز عن الأصل سقطت الوسيلة كالوضوء مع الصلاة والسعي مع الجمعة.
Or lying on his side with his face towards it, and the first is better. And if gesturing becomes impossible, it is postponed, and he does not gesture with his eyes, contrary to Zufar, nor with his eyebrows and his heart. And if bowing and prostration become impossible but not standing, he sits and gestures, and that is better than gesturing while standing. And our proof: Standing is a means to prostration, and prostration is the foundation, as evidenced by the fact that prostration is legally an act of worship without standing, as in the prostration of recitation, and standing is not prescribed as worship alone. If someone prostrates to other than Allah he disbelieves, but not so with standing. So when one is unable to perform the foundation, the means falls away, like wudu with prayer and the sa'i with Jumu'ah.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from a Hanafi commentary on the prayer of the sick. The base matn states several rulings: (1) A sick person may pray lying on his side or on his back, but the first is better because it aligns more with the qibla. (2) If even gesturing (with the head) is impossible, the prayer is postponed until ability returns, based on the Quranic principle of no excessive burden. (3) One should not gesture with eyes, eyebrows, or heart, contrary to Zufar (a Hanafi scholar), because only head-gesture is narratively established; substituting by opinion is not allowed. (4) If one can stand but cannot bow or prostrate, he sits and gestures, and that is better than standing and gesturing. This contradicts Zufar and the three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali), who require standing and gesturing because standing is a pillar. The Hanafi proof is that standing is a means (wasila) to prostration, which is the goal. Since prostration is valid without standing (as in sajda al-tilawa), and standing has no independent worship value, when the goal is impossible the means falls away, analogous to wudu being dropped when prayer is impossible or sa'i being dropped when Jumu'ah is missed. The commentary thus clarifies each sub-ruling with evidence and intra- and inter-madhhab disputes.

Key Terms
الإيماء (al-imā'): Gesturing with the head to indicate bowing and prostration when unable to perform them physically.
الوسيلة (al-wasīlah): A means to an act of worship; here, standing is a means to prostration.
الأبدال (al-abdāl): Substitutes; here, substituting head gesture with eye gesture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Stand and gesture with the head
B) Sit and gesture with the head
C) Lie on the side and gesture
D) Postpone the prayer until able to bow and prostrate

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes the goal of the act (prostration) over the means (standing), allowing the sick to sit and gesture for ease.

Restarting Prayer Upon Recovery from Gesturing
Intra-Hanafi dispute on first case: gesturer recovers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a person who gestures in prayer and then recovers, and distinguish the majority Hanafi position from Zufar's dissent.

Core Idea
A gesturing patient who recovers during prayer must restart; building is invalid.

This lesson examines the ruling when a sick person who prayed by gestures (unable to bow or prostrate) becomes healthy mid-prayer. The Hanafi school requires restarting the prayer, based on the principle that one who can fully bow cannot follow a gesturer. We will explore the evidence and the intra-Hanafi difference with Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and ruling
The case: a person begins prayer with gestures (īmāʾ) due to illness, then recovers the ability to perform rukūʿ and sujūd during the prayer. The ruling: his prayer is invalidated and he must restart (yastʾanif).
2
Understand the reasoning
The analogy to iqtidāʾ: just as a person who can bow/prostrate cannot follow a gesturing imam, building upon the gesture-based part of one's own prayer would mean following a weaker act with a stronger one (bināʾ al-qawī ʿalā al-ḍaʿīf).
The principle: لا يبني القوي على الضعيف (the strong does not build upon the weak).
3
Note the ikhtilāf with Zufar
Zufar allowed building in this case, but his view is not relied upon (marjūḥ). The three imams (Abū Ḥanīfa, Abū Yūsuf, Muḥammad) agree on restarting.
Zufar's dissent is mentioned but rejected.
4
Determine the muftā bihī
The muftā bihī (operative ruling for fatwa) is the majority view: restarting is obligatory (wājib).
The text does not explicitly mention 'wājib', but it is implied by 'batalat ṣalātuhu wa yajibu an yabtadiʾa bihā'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومومي صح في الصلاة استأنف وقاعد يركع ويسجد فصح فيها بنى قائما. قوله: ومومئ يعني من شرع صلاته بالإيماء لتعذر غيره فصح في أثناء صلاته وزال مرضه فقدر على الركوع والسجود بطلت صلاته ويجب عليه أن يبتدئ بها راكعا وساجدا ولا يبني على ما مضى لأنه لا يجوز اقتداء الراكع بالمومئ فكذا البناء لئلا يلزم بناء القوي على الضعيف وهذا على قول أئمتنا الثلاثة وفيه خلاف زفر. وأما من صلى قاعدا يركع ويسجد لعذر فقدر في أثناء صلاته على القيام يجوز له البناء عندهما خلافا لمحمد وهذا الخلاف مبني على جواز اقتداء القائم بالقاعد فعندهما يجوز وهو الأصح لما ثبت في السنن أن النبي صلى في مرض موته قاعدا والناس خلفه قيام فكذا يجوز البناء وعنده لا يجوز فكذا البناء.
And one who gestures, if he becomes healthy during the prayer, he restarts; and one who prays sitting and bowing and prostrating, if he becomes healthy during it, he builds upon it standing. His saying: 'one who gestures' means one who began his prayer with gestures due to the impossibility of other than that, then he becomes healthy during his prayer and his illness leaves, so he can bow and prostrate—his prayer is invalidated and he must begin it bowing and prostrating, and he does not build upon what has passed because it is not permissible for one who bows to follow one who gestures, and likewise building, to avoid building the strong upon the weak. This is according to the statement of our three imams, and there is a difference with Zufar. As for one who prayed sitting, bowing and prostrating due to an excuse, then becomes able to stand during his prayer, it is permissible for him to build according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Muhammad [according to one view]. This difference is based on the permissibility of a standing person following a sitting person. According to them it is permissible, and it is the most correct, because it is established in the Sunan that the Prophet prayed sitting during his illness leading to death while the people behind him were standing. So likewise building is permissible. According to him (Muhammad) it is not permissible, and likewise building.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on a sick person who recovers capacity during prayer. The matn gives two cases: First, if a person began praying with gestures (unable to bow/prostrate) and then recovers mid-prayer, his prayer is invalidated and he must restart from the beginning. This is because a person who can bow and prostrate cannot validly follow a person who only gestures (by analogy to leading prayer), and likewise building the strong upon the weak is invalid. This is the view of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (the three imams); Zufar differed, but his view is not relied upon. Second, if a person prayed sitting while performing actual bowing and prostrating (due to inability to stand) and then recovers the ability to stand, he may build upon his prayer—i.e., continue the same prayer standing—according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but Muhammad has a divergent opinion (perhaps a later view). The basis of this difference is whether a standing person may follow a sitting imam. The Sunan proves that the Prophet (peace be upon him) led prayer while sitting with people standing behind him, so building is analogically permissible. The text labels the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad as the most correct.

Key Terms
البناء (al-binā'): Continuing the same prayer after regaining ability, without restarting.
اقتداء (iqtidā'): Following an imam in prayer; here used analogically for building upon part of one's own prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (majority), what should a person do if they prayed with gestures due to illness and then recover fully during the prayer?
A) Continue the prayer standing
B) Restart the prayer from the beginning
C) Continue but repeat only the invalid parts
D) Make up the prayer later

Key Takeaway
A gesturing patient who recovers during prayer must restart; building is invalid.

Building Upon Prayer When a Sitting Patient Can Stand
Intra-Hanafi dispute on second case: sitting patient recovers standing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a sitting patient who recovers the ability to stand during prayer, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
A person who prayed sitting with actual bowing/prostration can build when recovering standing; the majority view is based on the Prophet's practice.

This lesson covers a second scenario: a person who began prayer sitting but performing actual rukūʿ and sujūd (due to inability to stand) then gains the ability to stand. The majority Hanafi position permits building, based on the permissibility of a standing person following a sitting imam, as proven by the Prophet's practice. We will examine the evidence and the dissenting view of Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second case and its ruling
The case: a person prays sitting with actual rukūʿ and sujūd (not gestures) due to an excuse, then recovers the ability to stand during the prayer. The ruling: according to Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad (in the relied-upon view), it is permissible (yajūz) for him to build – i.e., stand up and continue the prayer from where he left off.
2
Understand the basis: the iqtidāʾ analogy
The ruling is built on whether a standing person may follow a sitting imam. If it is permissible to follow a sitting imam while standing, then building one's own prayer from sitting to standing is analogous and thus permissible.
3
Examine the dalīl from the sunan
The proof cited: the Prophet (peace be upon him) led prayer while sitting in his final illness, and the companions stood behind him. This establishes the permissibility of a standing person following a sitting imam.
The hadith is well-known in the Sunan (e.g., Musnad Aḥmad, Ṣaḥīḥ al-Bukhārī).
4
Note the ikhtilāf with Muḥammad's other view
Muḥammad (al-Shaybānī) is reported to hold a different view, disallowing building in this case, because he does not permit a standing person to follow a sitting imam. However, the text says 'عندهما' (according to them both) and adds 'وهو الأصح' (and it is the most correct), indicating the majority view is the relied-upon position.
The sharh mentions that this difference is rooted in the iqtidāʾ issue.
5
Determine the muftā bihī
The muftā bihī is the view of Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad (the majority): building is permissible. The label 'al-aṣaḥḥ' (most correct) confirms this.

Source Text

Salah — Prayer

Recovery of Bowing/Prostration After Praying with Gestures
Ruling on invalidity of prayer when gesturing person recovers bowing/prostration ability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a gesturing (mūmī') worshipper recovers ability to bow and prostrate during prayer, and explain why the prayer becomes invalid (batil) according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Substitute fails when ability returns: gesturing invalidates past prayer.

Sometimes a sick person begins prayer using gestures due to inability to bow and prostrate. If during the prayer the excuse ceases and he becomes able to perform actual bowing and prostration, what happens to his prayer? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that the prayer becomes invalid and must be restarted, along with the reasoning and a minority intra-madhhab opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
A person starts prayer with gestures (īmā') because he cannot bow or prostrate. During the prayer, he recovers the ability to perform actual rukū' and sujūd.
Gestures are a substitute (badal) for the original acts.
2
State the ruling of the three imams
According to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the three imams), the prayer becomes invalid (bāṭil) and he must restart from the beginning. He does not build upon what has passed.
Zufar holds a different view (see dispute map).
3
Analyze the reasoning (qiyas)
The ruling is based on the principle that when a substitute is no longer needed, the original obligation must be fulfilled. Analogy: Like one who performs tayammum then finds water — wudu becomes obligatory and previous prayer is invalid. Likewise, bowing and prostrating are the essence of prayer; gestures are only a temporary concession.
Also: it is not valid for a bowing person to follow a gesturing imam; building would imply the stronger (actual bowing) being built on the weaker (gestures), which is impermissible.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Zufar holds that the prayer remains valid and the person may build upon it. The relied-upon position (muftā bihi) is the majority view of the three imams, as stated in the text: 'على قول أئمتنا الثلاثة'.
The text does not elaborate on Zufar's proof, but acknowledges the difference.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, if a sick person begins praying with gestures and recovers mid-prayer, he must stop and restart the prayer, now performing actual rukū' and sujūd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

لأن القعود أقرب من السجود، وهو المقصود ؛ لأنه غاية التّعظيم.

وقاعد يركع ويسجد فصح فيها بني قائماً.

شرعاً عبادة بدون القيام، كما في سجدةِ التَّلاوة ، والقيام لم يشرع عبادة وحده، ولو سجد لغير الله يكفر، ولا كذلك القيام ، فإذا عجز عن الأصل سقطت الوسيلة ؛ كالوضوء مع الصَّلاة، والسعي مع الجمعة.

[٢] قوله : ومومئ ؛ يعني من شرع صلاته بالإيماء، لتعذر غيره ، فصح في أثناء صلاته، وزال مرضُهُ فقَدِرَ على الركوع والسجودِ بطلت صلاته، ويجب عليه أن يبتدأ بها راكعاً وساجداً، ولا يبني على ما مضى ؛ لأنه لا يجوز اقتداء الراكع بالمومئ ، فكذا البناء ؛ لئلا يلزم بناء القوي على الضعيف، وهذا على قول أئمتنا الثلاثة، وفيه خلاف زفره .

Book of Prayer: Prayer of the Sick. And one who gestures [i.e., prays with gestures] and then becomes healthy during the prayer must restart. And one who prays sitting, bowing and prostrating, and then becomes healthy [able to stand] during the prayer may build [upon what he has already prayed] while standing. [1] Because sitting is closer to prostration, and prostration is the intended purpose because it is the ultimate form of humility. And 'one who gestures and becomes healthy during the prayer must restart' means he begins anew. 'And one who sits, bowing and prostrating, and then becomes healthy may build upon his prayer while standing.' 285 Legally, it is an act of worship without standing, as in the prostration of recitation. And standing alone is not prescribed as worship. If one prostrates to other than Allah, he disbelieves, but not so for standing. When one is unable to perform the original [obligation], the means falls away, like ablution for prayer and going out for Friday prayer. [1] His saying: 'And it is the intended purpose' i.e., prostration is the intended purpose from standing because it is the ultimate form of humility. This is in al-Hidayah and others, and there is a discussion mentioned in Fath al-Qadir. [2] His saying: 'And one who gestures' means one who began his prayer with gestures due to inability to do otherwise, then during his prayer he recovers and becomes able to bow and prostrate – his prayer is invalidated, and he must begin it anew bowing and prostrating. He does not build upon what has passed because it is not permissible for one bowing to follow one gesturing. Similarly, building upon it [is not allowed] lest a strong [prayer] be built upon a weak one. This is the saying of our three imams, and there is a difference of opinion from Zufar. As for one who prays sitting, bowing and prostrating due to an excuse, then during his prayer he becomes able to stand – he is permitted to build upon it according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], contrary to Muhammad [in another view]. This difference is based on the permissibility of a standing person following a sitting person. According to the two, it is permissible, and this is the most correct, as established in the Sunan: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) prayed sitting during his terminal illness while the people behind him were standing.' Thus, building upon it is permissible. According to him [Muhammad], it is not permissible, and so building is not permissible. (1) al-Hidayah 2:6. (2) Fath al-Qadir 2:6, and the discussion is: It may be denied that its prescription is exclusively for this, rather it is for this and for what is in itself of humility, as observed in practice, such that the arrogant dislike it. So when one of the two [acts of] humility is lost, it is required for what it contains. Evidence for negating this claim is that one who is able to sit, bow, and prostrate but not stand must sit even though there is not after it [i.e., after sitting] that ultimate end due to lack of being preceded by standing. (3) From Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her): 'Abu Bakr was praying standing, leading the prayer of the Prophet (peace be upon him), and the people were praying led by Abu Bakr, while the Prophet was sitting' (Sahih al-Bukhari 1:243, Sahih Muslim 1:301, Sahih Ibn Khuzaymah 1:126, and others).
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands on two rulings from the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding change in capacity during prayer. The first ruling: someone who begins prayer with gestures due to inability, then recovers the ability to bow and prostrate during the prayer – his prayer becomes invalid, and he must restart from the beginning. The reason given is that bowing/prostrating is the essential act of worship, and gestures are a substitute. When the excuse ceases, the substitute falls away, analogous to how one who cannot perform wudu does tayammum, but when water is found, wudu becomes obligatory. The second ruling: someone who prays sitting with actual bowing and prostrating, then recovers the ability to stand – according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, he may continue from where he left off while standing. This is based on the permissibility of a standing person following a sitting imam, as established by the hadith of the Prophet's illness where he sat and the companions stood behind him. Imam Muhammad reportedly differed in one narration, holding that building is not allowed because the stronger action cannot be built on the weaker, similar to the first case. However, the text declares the view of the two as 'al-asah' (most correct). The discussion also notes that Imam Zufar disagreed with the first ruling, opining that the prayer remains valid. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the consensus of the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) for the first, and the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad for the second.

Key Terms
مومئ (mūmī'): One who prays using gestures (إيماء) instead of actual bowing and prostration due to inability.
بناء (binā'): Continuing the prayer from the point of interruption rather than restarting; building upon the existing prayer.
بدل (badal): Substitute – when an original act cannot be performed, a substitute (like gestures for bowing) is allowed. Once ability returns, substitute falls.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), what happens when a person who starts prayer with gestures recovers the ability to bow and prostrate during the prayer?
A) He may continue from where he left off, now bowing and prostrating.
B) He must restart the prayer from the beginning.
C) He must continue sitting and bow/prostrate while sitting.
D) His prayer becomes valid only if he recovers before the first rak'ah.

Key Takeaway
When ability to perform actual bowing/prostration returns during prayer, the substitute of gestures falls away, voiding the entire prayer.

Recovery of Standing After Praying Sitting with Bowing/Prostration
Ruling on permissibility of building prayer when a sitting person recovers standing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a person who recovers the ability to stand during prayer may build upon his prayer, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and identify the intra-Hanafi difference.

Core Idea
Recovering standing is like gaining a condition: building is allowed due to the hadith of the Prophet leading while sitting.

What if a person starts prayer sitting, but actually performs bowing and prostration, and then recovers the ability to stand? The Hanafi school, led by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, allows him to continue from where he left off while standing. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the disputed evidence, and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
A person begins praying sitting due to an excuse, but he performs actual bowing and prostration (not gestures). During the prayer, he recovers the ability to stand.
He is not a mūmī'; he is a qā'id (sitter) who bows and prostrates properly.
2
State the ruling of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad)
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the two, عندهما), he may build upon his prayer and continue standing. The prayer does not become invalid.
In one narration, Imam Muhammad disagreed and required restart.
3
Trace the proof
The proof is a hadith: During the Prophet's terminal illness, he led prayer while sitting, and the companions stood behind him. This proves that a standing person can validly follow a sitting imam. By analogy, building a standing part on a sitting part is permissible.
Bukhari and Muslim narrate this.
4
Identify the opposing view and its basis
Imam Muhammad (in one view) and Imam Zufar argue that building is not allowed, because it would mean building the stronger (standing) on the weaker (sitting), similar to the first ruling where building on gestures is invalid.
This is a qiyas argument.
5
Determine the tarjih
The text declares the view of the two as 'al-aṣaḥ' (most correct). The hadith overrides the analogy. Thus, the muftā bihi is that building is permitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة صلاة المريض
ومومي صح في الصَّلاةِ استأنف، وقاعد يركع ويسجد فصح فيها بَنَى قائماً.
[۱]
لأن القعود أقرب من السجود، وهو المقصود ؛ لأنه غاية التّعظيم.
ومومي صح في الصلاة استأنف) : أي ابتدأ.
وقاعد يركع ويسجد فصح فيها بني قائماً.
٢٨٥
شرعاً عبادة بدون القيام، كما في سجدةِ التَّلاوة ، والقيام لم يشرع عبادة وحده، ولو سجد لغير الله يكفر، ولا كذلك القيام ، فإذا عجز عن الأصل سقطت الوسيلة ؛ كالوضوء مع الصَّلاة، والسعي مع الجمعة.
